<td style="width:1.0em; padding:0.4em 0 0 0.6em;">a.</td>
<td colspan="2" style="padding-left:0;text-align:left;"><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_name_transl"><b><a href="#ref_name_transl">^</a></b> <i><span lang="grc">Βασιλεία Ῥωμαίων</span></i> may be transliterated in Latin as <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Ancient Greek transliteration">Basileia Rhōmaiōn</span></i>, meaning <i>Roman Empire</i>.</span></td>
</tr>
<tr class="mergedrow" style="font-size:85%;">
<td style="width:1.0em; padding:0 0 0 0.6em;">b.</td>
<td colspan="2" style="padding-left:0;text-align:left;"><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_lifespan"><b><a href="#ref_lifespan">^</a></b> Between 1204 and 1261 there was an interregnum when the Empire was divided into the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Nicaea" title="Empire of Nicaea">Empire of Nicaea</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Trebizond" title="Empire of Trebizond">Empire of Trebizond</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Despotate_of_Epirus" title="Despotate of Epirus">Despotate of Epirus</a>, which were all contenders for rule of the Empire. The Empire of Nicaea is considered the legitimate continuation of the Byzantine Empire because they managed to re-take Constantinople.</span></td>
</tr>
<tr class="mergedbottomrow" style="font-size:85%;">
<td style="width:1.0em; padding:0 0 0 0.6em;">c.</td>
<td colspan="2" style="padding-left:0;text-align:left;"><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_population"><b><a href="#ref_population">^</a></b> See <a href="/wiki/Population_of_the_Byzantine_Empire" title="Population of the Byzantine Empire">Population of the Byzantine Empire</a> for more detailed figures taken provided by McEvedy and Jones, <i>Atlas of World Population History</i>, 1978, as well as Angeliki E. Laiou, <i>The Economic History of Byzantium</i>, 2002.</span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b>Byzantine Empire</b>, also referred to as the <b>Eastern Roman Empire</b>, was the continuation of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> in the East during <a href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, when its capital city was <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>, which had been founded as <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a>). It survived the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">fragmentation and fall of the Western Roman Empire</a> in the 5th century AD and continued to exist for an additional thousand years until it fell to the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Turks</a> in 1453.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> During most of its existence, the empire was the most powerful economic, cultural, and military force in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>. Both "Byzantine Empire" and "Eastern Roman Empire" are historiographical terms created after the end of the realm; its citizens continued to refer to their empire as the <i>Roman Empire</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">Βασιλεία τῶν Ῥωμαίων</span>, <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Greek" title="Romanization of Greek">tr.</a> <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Ancient Greek transliteration"><i>Basileia tôn Rhōmaiōn</i></span>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <span lang="la"><i>Imperium Romanum</i></span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKazhdanEpstein19851_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKazhdanEpstein19851-3">[3]</a></sup> or <i>Romania</i> (<span lang="grc">Ῥωμανία</span>), and to themselves as "Romans".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMillar20062.2C_15James20105Freeman1999431.2C_435.E2.80.93437.2C_459-462BaynesMoss1948xxOstrogorsky196927Kaldellis20072.E2.80.933KazhdanConstable198212Norwich1998383_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMillar20062.2C_15James20105Freeman1999431.2C_435.E2.80.93437.2C_459-462BaynesMoss1948xxOstrogorsky196927Kaldellis20072.E2.80.933KazhdanConstable198212Norwich1998383-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several signal events from the 4th to 6th centuries mark the period of transition during which the Roman Empire's <a href="/wiki/Greek_East_and_Latin_West" title="Greek East and Latin West">Greek East and Latin West</a> divided. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">Constantine I</a> (r. 324–337) reorganised the empire, made Constantinople the new capital, and legalised <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>. Under <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a> (r. 379–395), Christianity became the Empire's official <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">state religion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christian_persecution_of_paganism_under_Theodosius_I" title="Christian persecution of paganism under Theodosius I">other religious practices were proscribed</a>. Finally, under the reign of <a href="/wiki/Heraclius" title="Heraclius">Heraclius</a> (r. 610–641), the Empire's military and administration were restructured and adopted Greek for official use instead of Latin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOstrogorsky1969105.E2.80.93107.2C_109Norwich199897Haywood20012.17.2C_3.06.2C_3.15_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOstrogorsky1969105.E2.80.93107.2C_109Norwich199897Haywood20012.17.2C_3.06.2C_3.15-5">[5]</a></sup> Thus, although the Roman state continued and Roman state traditions were maintained, modern historians distinguish Byzantium from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Rome</a> insofar as it was centred on Constantinople, oriented towards Greek rather than Latin culture, and characterised by <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Orthodox Christianity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMillar20062.2C_15James20105Freeman1999431.2C_435.E2.80.93437.2C_459.E2.80.93462BaynesMoss1948xxOstrogorsky196927Kaldellis20072.E2.80.933KazhdanConstable198212Norwich1998383_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMillar20062.2C_15James20105Freeman1999431.2C_435.E2.80.93437.2C_459.E2.80.93462BaynesMoss1948xxOstrogorsky196927Kaldellis20072.E2.80.933KazhdanConstable198212Norwich1998383-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>The borders of the Empire evolved significantly over its existence, as it went through several cycles of decline and recovery. During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> (r. 527–565), the Empire reached its greatest extent after reconquering much of the historically Roman western <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean coast</a>, including North Africa, Italy, and Rome itself, which it held for two more centuries. During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Maurice_(emperor)" title="Maurice (emperor)">Maurice</a> (r. 582–602), the Empire's eastern frontier was expanded and the north stabilised. However, his assassination caused the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Sasanian_War_of_602%E2%80%93628" title="Byzantine–Sasanian War of 602–628">Byzantine–Sasanian War of 602–628</a>, which exhausted the Empire's resources and contributed to major territorial losses during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" title="Muslim conquests">Muslim conquests</a> of the seventh century. In a matter of years the Empire lost its richest provinces, Egypt and Syria, to the Arabs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_dynasty" title="Macedonian dynasty">Macedonian dynasty</a> (10th–11th centuries), the Empire again expanded and experienced the two-century long <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_Renaissance" title="Macedonian Renaissance">Macedonian Renaissance</a>, which came to an end with the loss of much of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Seljuq_dynasty" title="Seljuq dynasty">Seljuk Turks</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Manzikert" title="Battle of Manzikert">Battle of Manzikert</a> in 1071. This battle opened the way for the Turks to settle in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>.</p>
<p>The Empire recovered again during the <a href="/wiki/Komnenian_restoration" title="Komnenian restoration">Komnenian restoration</a>, such that by the 12th century Constantinople was the largest and wealthiest European city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> However, it was delivered a mortal blow during the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Fourth Crusade</a>, when Constantinople was sacked in 1204 and the territories that the Empire formerly governed <a href="/wiki/Partitio_terrarum_imperii_Romaniae" title="Partitio terrarum imperii Romaniae">were divided</a> into competing Byzantine Greek and <a href="/wiki/Frankokratia" title="Frankokratia">Latin realms</a>. Despite the eventual <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Palaiologos_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Palaiologos dynasty">recovery of Constantinople</a> in 1261, the Byzantine Empire remained only one of several small rival states in the area for the final two centuries of its existence. Its remaining territories were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Ottoman_Wars" title="Byzantine–Ottoman Wars">progressively annexed by the Ottomans</a> over the 15th century. The <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">Fall of Constantinople</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> in 1453 finally ended the Byzantine Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Names_of_the_Greeks" title="Names of the Greeks">Names of the Greeks</a></div>
<p>The first use of the term "Byzantine" to label the later years of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> was in 1557, when the German historian <a href="/wiki/Hieronymus_Wolf" title="Hieronymus Wolf">Hieronymus Wolf</a> published his work <i>Corpus Historiæ Byzantinæ</i>, a collection of historical sources. The term comes from "Byzantium", the name of the city of Constantinople before it became Constantine's capital. This older name of the city would rarely be used from this point onward except in historical or poetic contexts. The publication in 1648 of the <i>Byzantine du Louvre</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Corpus_Scriptorum_Historiae_Byzantinae" title="Corpus Scriptorum Historiae Byzantinae">Corpus Scriptorum Historiae Byzantinae</a></i>), and in 1680 of <a href="/wiki/Charles_du_Fresne,_sieur_du_Cange" title="Charles du Fresne, sieur du Cange">Du Cange</a>'s <i>Historia Byzantina</i> further popularised the use of "Byzantine" among French authors, such as <a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> However, it was not until the mid-19th century that the term came into general use in the Western world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Byzantine Empire was known to its inhabitants as the "Roman Empire", the "Empire of the Romans" (Latin: <i>Imperium Romanum</i>, <i>Imperium Romanorum</i>; Greek: <span lang="grc">Βασιλεία τῶν Ῥωμαίων</span> <i>Basileia tōn Rhōmaiōn</i>, <span lang="grc">Ἀρχὴ τῶν Ῥωμαίων</span> <i>Archē tōn Rhōmaiōn</i>), "Romania" (Latin: <i>Romania</i>; Greek: <span lang="grc">Ῥωμανία</span> <i>Rhōmania</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[n 1]</a></sup> the "Roman Republic" (Latin: <i>Res Publica Romana</i>; Greek: <span lang="grc">Πολιτεία τῶν Ῥωμαίων</span> <i>Politeia tōn Rhōmaiōn</i>), <i>Graikia</i> (Greek: Γραικία), and also as <i>Rhōmais</i> (Greek: <span lang="grc">Ῥωμαΐς</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> The inhabitants called themselves <i>Romaioi</i> and <i>Graikoi</i>, and even as late as the 19th century Greeks typically referred to their modern language as <i><a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">Romaika</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">Graikika</a></i>.</p>
<p>Although the Byzantine Empire had a multi-ethnic character during most of its history<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> and preserved <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">Romano-Hellenistic</a> traditions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> it became identified by its western and northern contemporaries with its increasingly predominant <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Greeks" title="Byzantine Greeks">Greek element</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> The occasional use of the term "Empire of the Greeks" (Latin: <i>Imperium Graecorum</i>) in the West to refer to the Eastern Roman Empire and of the Byzantine Emperor as <i>Imperator Graecorum</i> (Emperor of the Greeks)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> were also used to separate it from the prestige of the Roman Empire within the new kingdoms of the West.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>The authority of the Byzantine emperor as the legitimate Roman emperor was challenged by the coronation of <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> as <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor"><i>Imperator Augustus</i></a> by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_III" title="Pope Leo III">Pope Leo III</a> in the year 800. Needing Charlemagne's support in his struggle against his enemies in Rome, Leo used the lack of a male occupant of the throne of the Roman Empire at the time to claim that it was vacant and that he could therefore crown a new Emperor himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>No such distinction existed in the Islamic and Slavic worlds, where the Empire was more straightforwardly seen as the continuation of the Roman Empire. In the Islamic world, the Roman Empire was known primarily as <i><a href="/wiki/R%C3%BBm" title="Rûm">Rûm</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> The name <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rum_millet" title="Rum millet">millet-i Rûm</a>, or "<i>Roman nation,</i>" was used by the Ottomans through the 20th century to refer to the former subjects of the Byzantine Empire, that is, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_Christian" title="Orthodox Christian">Orthodox Christian</a> community within Ottoman realms.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Byzantine_Empire" title="History of the Byzantine Empire">History of the Byzantine Empire</a></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raphael_Baptism_Constantine.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="740" data-file-width="1191" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Raphael_Baptism_Constantine.jpg/250px-Raphael_Baptism_Constantine.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Raphael_Baptism_Constantine.jpg/375px-Raphael_Baptism_Constantine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Raphael_Baptism_Constantine.jpg/500px-Raphael_Baptism_Constantine.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Raphael_Baptism_Constantine.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The Baptism of Constantine</i> painted by <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>'s pupils (1520–1524, <a href="/wiki/Fresco" title="Fresco">fresco</a>, Vatican City, <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_Palace" title="Apostolic Palace">Apostolic Palace</a>); <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eusebius_of_Caesarea" title="Eusebius of Caesarea">Eusebius of Caesarea</a> records that (as <a href="/wiki/Baptism_in_early_Christianity#Theology" title="Baptism in early Christianity">was common among converts of early Christianity</a>) Constantine delayed receiving <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a> until shortly before his death<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Roman_army" title="Roman army">Roman army</a> succeeded in conquering many territories covering the entire Mediterranean region and coastal regions in <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">southwestern Europe</a> and north Africa. These territories were home to many different cultural groups, both urban populations and rural populations. Generally speaking, the eastern Mediterranean provinces were more urbanised than the western, having previously been united under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedonian_Empire" title="Macedonian Empire">Macedonian Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hellenization" title="Hellenization">Hellenised</a> by the influence of Greek culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ostrogorsky_1959_21_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ostrogorsky_1959_21-24">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>The West also suffered more heavily from the instability of the 3rd century AD. This distinction between the established Hellenised East and the younger Latinised West persisted and became increasingly important in later centuries, leading to a gradual estrangement of the two worlds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ostrogorsky_1959_21_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ostrogorsky_1959_21-24">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decentralization_of_power">Decentralization of power</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Byzantium_under_the_Constantinian_and_Valentinian_dynasties" title="Byzantium under the Constantinian and Valentinian dynasties">Byzantium under the Constantinian and Valentinian dynasties</a></div>
<p>To maintain control and improve administration, various schemes to divide the work of the Roman Emperor by sharing it between individuals were tried between 285 and 324, from 337 to 350, from 364 to 392, and again between 395 and 480. Although the administrative subdivisions varied, they generally involved a division of labour between East and West. Each division was a form of power-sharing (or even job-sharing), for the ultimate <i>imperium</i> was not divisible and therefore the empire remained legally one state—although the co-emperors often saw each other as rivals or enemies.</p>
<p>In 293, emperor <a href="/wiki/Diocletian" title="Diocletian">Diocletian</a> created a new administrative system (the <a href="/wiki/Tetrarchy" title="Tetrarchy">tetrarchy</a>), to guarantee security in all endangered regions of his Empire. He associated himself with a co-emperor (<i><a href="/wiki/Augustus_(honorific)" title="Augustus (honorific)">Augustus</a></i>), and each co-emperor then adopted a young colleague given the title of <i><a href="/wiki/Caesar_(title)" title="Caesar (title)">Caesar</a></i>, to share in their rule and eventually to succeed the senior partner. The tetrarchy collapsed, however, in 313 and a few years later Constantine I reunited the two administrative divisions of the Empire as sole Augustus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuhoff_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhoff-25">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recentralisation">Recentralisation</span></h3>
<p>In 330, <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Constantine</a> moved the <a href="/wiki/Seat_of_government" title="Seat of government">seat of the Empire</a> to <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, which he founded as a second Rome on the site of Byzantium, a city strategically located on the trade routes between Europe and Asia and between the Mediterranean and the Black Sea. Constantine introduced important changes into the Empire's military, monetary, civil and religious institutions. As regards his economic policies in particular, he has been accused by certain scholars of "reckless fiscality", but the gold <a href="/wiki/Solidus_(coin)" title="Solidus (coin)">solidus</a> he introduced became a stable currency that transformed the economy and promoted development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esler-1081_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-esler-1081-26">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under Constantine, Christianity did not become the exclusive religion of the state, but enjoyed imperial preference, because <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great_and_Christianity" title="Constantine the Great and Christianity">the emperor supported it with generous privileges</a>. Constantine established the principle that emperors could not settle questions of doctrine on their own, but should summon instead <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_council" title="Ecumenical council">general ecclesiastical councils</a> for that purpose. His convening of both the <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Arles" title="Synod of Arles">Synod of Arles</a> and the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">First Council of Nicaea</a> indicated his interest in the unity of the Church, and showcased his claim to be its head.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B163_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-B163-27">[26]</a></sup> The rise of Christianity was briefly interrupted on the accession of the emperor <a href="/wiki/Julian_(emperor)" title="Julian (emperor)">Julian</a> in 361, who made a determined effort to restore polytheism throughout the empire and was thus dubbed "Julian the Apostate" by the Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> However this was reversed when Julian was killed in battle in 363.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG/250px-Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG/375px-Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG/500px-Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Restored section of the Theodosian Walls.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a> (379-395) was the last Emperor to rule both the Eastern and Western halves of the Empire. In 391 and 392 he <a href="/wiki/Christian_persecution_of_paganism_under_Theodosius_I" title="Christian persecution of paganism under Theodosius I">issued a series of edicts essentially banning pagan religion</a>. Pagan festivals and sacrifices were banned, as was access to all pagan temples and places of worship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> The last <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Olympic_Games" title="Ancient Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a> are believed to have been held in 393.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> In 395, <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a> bequeathed the imperial office jointly to his sons: <a href="/wiki/Arcadius" title="Arcadius">Arcadius</a> in the East and <a href="/wiki/Honorius_(emperor)" title="Honorius (emperor)">Honorius</a> in the West, once again dividing Imperial administration. In the 5th century the Eastern part of the empire was largely spared the difficulties faced by the West—due in part to a more established urban culture and greater financial resources, which allowed it to placate invaders with <a href="/wiki/Tribute" title="Tribute">tribute</a> and pay foreign mercenaries. This success allowed <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_II" title="Theodosius II">Theodosius II</a> to focus on the <a href="/wiki/Codex_Theodosianus" title="Codex Theodosianus">codification of Roman law</a> and further fortification of <a href="/wiki/Walls_of_Constantinople" title="Walls of Constantinople">the walls of Constantinople</a>, which left the city impervious to most attacks until 1204.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> Large portions of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theodosian_Walls" title="Theodosian Walls">Theodosian Walls</a> are preserved to the present day.</p>
<p>To fend off the <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Huns</a>, Theodosius had to pay an enormous annual tribute to <a href="/wiki/Attila" title="Attila">Attila</a>. His successor, <a href="/wiki/Marcian" title="Marcian">Marcian</a>, refused to continue to pay the tribute, but Attila had already diverted his attention to the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">West</a>. After his death in 453, the <a href="/wiki/Huns#Unified_Empire_under_Attila" title="Huns">Hunnic Empire</a> collapsed, and many of the remaining Huns were often hired as mercenaries by Constantinople.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loss_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire">Loss of the Western Roman Empire</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roman_Empire_460_AD.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="396" data-file-width="694" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Roman_Empire_460_AD.png/250px-Roman_Empire_460_AD.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Roman_Empire_460_AD.png/375px-Roman_Empire_460_AD.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Roman_Empire_460_AD.png/500px-Roman_Empire_460_AD.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roman_Empire_460_AD.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Roman Empire during the reigns of Leo I <i>(east)</i> and <a href="/wiki/Majorian" title="Majorian">Majorian</a> <i>(west)</i> in 460 AD. Roman rule in the west would last less than two more decades, whereas the territory of the east would remain static until the reconquests of Justinian I.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the fall of Attila, the Eastern Empire enjoyed a period of peace, while the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">Western Empire</a> deteriorated due to continuing migration and expansion by the <a href="/wiki/Germania" title="Germania">Germanic nations</a> (its end is usually dated in 476 when the Germanic Roman general <a href="/wiki/Odoacer" title="Odoacer">Odoacer</a> deposed the usurper Western Emperor <a href="/wiki/Romulus_Augustulus" title="Romulus Augustulus">Romulus Augustulus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup>).</p>
<p>In 480 with the death of the Western Emperor <a href="/wiki/Julius_Nepos" title="Julius Nepos">Julius Nepos</a>, Eastern Emperor <a href="/wiki/Zeno_(emperor)" title="Zeno (emperor)">Zeno</a> became sole Emperor of the empire. Odoacer, now ruler of Italy, was nominally Zeno's subordinate but acted with complete autonomy, eventually providing support to a rebellion against the Emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burns_1991_65.2C_76.E2.80.9377.2C_86.E2.80.9387_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burns_1991_65.2C_76.E2.80.9377.2C_86.E2.80.9387-35">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Zeno negotiated with the invading <a href="/wiki/Ostrogoths" title="Ostrogoths">Ostrogoths</a>, who had settled in <a href="/wiki/Moesia" title="Moesia">Moesia</a>, convincing the Gothic king <a href="/wiki/Theoderic_the_Great" title="Theoderic the Great">Theodoric</a> to depart for Italy as <i>magister militum per Italiam</i> ("commander in chief for Italy") with the aim of deposing Odoacer. By urging Theodoric to conquer Italy, Zeno rid the Eastern Empire of an unruly subordinate (Odoacer) and moved another (Theodoric) further from the heart of the Empire. After Odoacer's defeat in 493, Theodoric ruled Italy de facto, although he was never recognised by the eastern emperors as "king" (<i>rex</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burns_1991_65.2C_76.E2.80.9377.2C_86.E2.80.9387_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Burns_1991_65.2C_76.E2.80.9377.2C_86.E2.80.9387-35">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 491, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anastasius_I_(emperor)" title="Anastasius I (emperor)">Anastasius I</a>, an aged civil officer of Roman origin, became Emperor, but it was not until 497 that the forces of the new emperor effectively took the measure of <a href="/wiki/Isaurian_War" title="Isaurian War">Isaurian resistance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> Anastasius revealed himself as an energetic reformer and an able administrator. He perfected Constantine I's coinage system by definitively setting the weight of the copper <i><a href="/wiki/Follis" title="Follis">follis</a></i>, the coin used in most everyday transactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> He also reformed the tax system and permanently abolished the <a href="/wiki/Collatio_lustralis" title="Collatio lustralis">chrysargyron</a> tax. The State Treasury contained the enormous sum of 320,000 lb (150,000 kg) of gold when Anastasius died in 518.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Justinian_dynasty">Justinian dynasty</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Justinian_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Justinian dynasty">Byzantine Empire under the Justinian dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Justinian.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="340" data-file-width="267" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Justinian.jpg/200px-Justinian.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/91/Justinian.jpg 1.5x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meister_von_San_Vitale_in_Ravenna_008.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2045" data-file-width="1576" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Meister_von_San_Vitale_in_Ravenna_008.jpg/200px-Meister_von_San_Vitale_in_Ravenna_008.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Meister_von_San_Vitale_in_Ravenna_008.jpg/300px-Meister_von_San_Vitale_in_Ravenna_008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Meister_von_San_Vitale_in_Ravenna_008.jpg/400px-Meister_von_San_Vitale_in_Ravenna_008.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

<a href="/wiki/Theodora_(6th_century)" title="Theodora (6th century)">Theodora</a>, Justinian's wife, depicted on the mosaics of the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_San_Vitale" title="Basilica of San Vitale">Basilica of San Vitale</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Justinian dynasty was founded by <a href="/wiki/Justin_I" title="Justin I">Justin I</a>, who though illiterate, rose through the ranks of the military to become Emperor in 518.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> He was succeeded by his nephew <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> in 527, who may already have exerted effective control during Justin's reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M290_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-M290-40">[39]</a></sup> One of the most important figures of late antiquity and possibly the last Roman emperor to speak Latin as a first language,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> Justinian's rule constitutes a distinct epoch, marked by the ambitious but only partly realized <i>renovatio imperii</i>, or "restoration of the Empire".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> His wife <a href="/wiki/Theodora_(6th_century)" title="Theodora (6th century)">Theodora</a> was particularly influential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 529, Justinian appointed a ten-man commission chaired by <a href="/wiki/John_the_Cappadocian" title="John the Cappadocian">John the Cappadocian</a> to revise Roman law and create a new codification of laws and jurists' extracts, known as the "<i><a href="/wiki/Corpus_Juris_Civilis" title="Corpus Juris Civilis">Corpus Juris Civilis</a></i>"or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Justinian_Code" title="Justinian Code">Justinian Code</a>. In 534, the <i>Corpus</i> was updated and, along with the <a href="/wiki/Novellae_Constitutiones" title="Novellae Constitutiones">enactments promulgated by Justinian after 534</a>, formed the system of law used for most of the rest of the Byzantine era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> The <i>Corpus</i> forms the basis of <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a> of many modern states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 532, attempting to secure his eastern frontier, Justinian signed a peace treaty with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khosrau_I" title="Khosrau I">Khosrau I of Persia</a> agreeing to pay a large annual tribute to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanids</a>. In the same year, he survived a revolt in Constantinople (the <a href="/wiki/Nika_riots" title="Nika riots">Nika riots</a>), which solidified his power but ended with the deaths of a reported 30,000 to 35,000 rioters on his orders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> The western conquests began in 533, as Justinian sent his general <a href="/wiki/Belisarius" title="Belisarius">Belisarius</a> to reclaim the former province of <a href="/wiki/Africa_(Roman_province)" title="Africa (Roman province)">Africa</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Vandals" title="Vandals">Vandals</a> who had been in control since 429 with their capital at Carthage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> Their success came with surprising ease, but it was not until 548 that the major local tribes were subdued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ev_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ev-48">[47]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Ostrogothic_Kingdom" title="Ostrogothic Kingdom">Ostrogothic Italy</a>, the deaths of Theodoric, his nephew and heir <a href="/wiki/Athalaric" title="Athalaric">Athalaric</a>, and his daughter <a href="/wiki/Amalasuntha" title="Amalasuntha">Amalasuntha</a> had left her murderer, <a href="/wiki/Theodahad" title="Theodahad">Theodahad</a> (r. 534–536), on the throne despite his weakened authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B180-216_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-B180-216-49">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 535, a small Byzantine expedition to <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> met with easy success, but the Goths soon stiffened their resistance, and victory did not come until 540, when Belisarius captured <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a>, after successful sieges of <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a> and Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B180-216_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-B180-216-49">[48]</a></sup> In 535–536, Theodahad sent <a href="/wiki/Pope_Agapetus_I" title="Pope Agapetus I">Pope Agapetus I</a> to Constantinople to request the removal of Byzantine forces from Sicily, <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia_(Roman_province)" title="Dalmatia (Roman province)">Dalmatia</a>, and Italy. Although Agapetus failed in his mission to sign a peace with Justinian, he succeeded in having the <a href="/wiki/Monophysitism" title="Monophysitism">Monophysite</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_Anthimus_I_of_Constantinople" title="Patriarch Anthimus I of Constantinople">Patriarch Anthimus I of Constantinople</a> denounced, despite empress <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theodora_(wife_of_Justinian_I)" title="Theodora (wife of Justinian I)">Theodora</a>'s support and protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maas278T187_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maas278T187-50">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Ostrogoths were soon reunited under the command of King <a href="/wiki/Totila" title="Totila">Totila</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Rome_(546)" title="Sack of Rome (546)">captured Rome</a> in 546. Belisarius, who had been sent back to Italy in 544, was eventually recalled to Constantinople in 549.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B236-258_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-B236-258-51">[50]</a></sup> The arrival of the Armenian eunuch <a href="/wiki/Narses" title="Narses">Narses</a> in Italy (late 551) with an army of 35,000 men marked another shift in Gothic fortunes. Totila was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Taginae" title="Battle of Taginae">Battle of Taginae</a> and his successor, <a href="/wiki/Teia" title="Teia">Teia</a>, was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mons_Lactarius" title="Battle of Mons Lactarius">Battle of Mons Lactarius</a> (October 552). Despite continuing resistance from a few Gothic garrisons and two subsequent invasions by the <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alemanni" title="Alemanni">Alemanni</a>, the war for the Italian peninsula was at an end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B259-281_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-B259-281-52">[51]</a></sup> In 551, <a href="/wiki/Athanagild" title="Athanagild">Athanagild</a>, a noble from <a href="/wiki/Visigoths" title="Visigoths">Visigothic</a> <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispania</a>, sought Justinian's help in a rebellion against the king, and the emperor dispatched a force under <a href="/wiki/Liberius_(praetorian_prefect)" title="Liberius (praetorian prefect)">Liberius</a>, a successful military commander. The empire held on to a small slice of the <a href="/wiki/Spania" title="Spania">Iberian Peninsula</a> coast until the reign of Heraclius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B86-288_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-B86-288-53">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the east, the Roman–Persian Wars continued until 561 when the envoys of Justinian and Khosrau agreed on a 50-year peace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> By the mid-550s, Justinian had won victories in most theatres of operation, with the notable exception of the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, which were subjected to repeated incursions from the <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavs</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gepids" title="Gepids">Gepids</a>. Tribes of <a href="/wiki/Serbs" title="Serbs">Serbs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Croats" title="Croats">Croats</a> were later resettled in the northwestern Balkans, during the reign of Heraclius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> Justinian called Belisarius out of retirement and defeated the new Hunnish threat. The strengthening of the Danube fleet caused the <a href="/wiki/Kutrigurs" title="Kutrigurs">Kutrigur</a> Huns to withdraw and they agreed to a treaty that allowed safe passage back across the Danube.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although polytheism had been <a href="/wiki/Decline_of_Greco-Roman_polytheism" title="Decline of Greco-Roman polytheism">suppressed by the state</a> since at least the time of Constantine in the 4th century, traditional Greco-Roman culture was still influential in the Eastern empire in the 6th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> Philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/John_Philoponus" title="John Philoponus">John Philoponus</a> drew on <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">neoplatonic</a> ideas in addition to Christian thought and <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricism</a>. Nevertheless, <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_philosophy" title="Hellenistic philosophy">Hellenistic philosophy</a> began to be gradually supplanted by or amalgamated into newer <a href="/wiki/Christian_philosophy" title="Christian philosophy">Christian philosophy</a>. The closure of the <a href="/wiki/Platonic_Academy" title="Platonic Academy">Platonic Academy</a> in 529 was a notable turning point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> Hymns written by <a href="/wiki/Romanos_the_Melodist" title="Romanos the Melodist">Romanos the Melodist</a> marked the development of the <a href="/wiki/Divine_Liturgy" title="Divine Liturgy">Divine Liturgy</a>, while the architects <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Miletus" title="Isidore of Miletus">Isidore of Miletus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anthemius_of_Tralles" title="Anthemius of Tralles">Anthemius of Tralles</a> worked to complete the new Church of the <a href="/wiki/Sophia_(wisdom)" title="Sophia (wisdom)">Holy Wisdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, which was designed to replace an older church destroyed during the Nika Revolt. Completed in 537, the Hagia Sophia stands today as one of the major monuments of Byzantine architectural history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> During the 6th and 7th centuries, the Empire was struck by a <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Justinian" title="Plague of Justinian">series of epidemics</a>, which greatly devastated the population and contributed to a significant economic decline and a weakening of the Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roman_Empire_600_AD.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="396" data-file-width="694" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Roman_Empire_600_AD.PNG/250px-Roman_Empire_600_AD.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Roman_Empire_600_AD.PNG/375px-Roman_Empire_600_AD.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Roman_Empire_600_AD.PNG/500px-Roman_Empire_600_AD.PNG 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roman_Empire_600_AD.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Eastern Roman Empire in 600 AD during the reign of Emperor Maurice.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After Justinian died in 565, his successor, <a href="/wiki/Justin_II" title="Justin II">Justin II</a> refused to pay the large tribute to the Persians. Meanwhile, the Germanic <a href="/wiki/Lombards" title="Lombards">Lombards</a> invaded Italy; by the end of the century only a third of Italy was in Byzantine hands. Justin's successor, <a href="/wiki/Tiberius_II_Constantine" title="Tiberius II Constantine">Tiberius II</a>, choosing between his enemies, awarded subsidies to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avars_(Carpathians)" title="Avars (Carpathians)">Avars</a> while taking military action against the Persians. Though Tiberius' general, <a href="/wiki/Maurice_(emperor)" title="Maurice (emperor)">Maurice</a>, led an effective campaign on the eastern frontier, subsidies failed to restrain the Avars. They captured the Balkan fortress of <a href="/wiki/Sirmium" title="Sirmium">Sirmium</a> in 582, while the Slavs began to make inroads across the Danube.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Louth_2005_113.E2.80.93115_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Louth_2005_113.E2.80.93115-61">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Maurice, who meanwhile succeeded Tiberius, intervened in a Persian civil war, placed the legitimate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khosrau_II" title="Khosrau II">Khosrau II</a> back on the throne and married his daughter to him. Maurice's treaty with his new brother-in-law enlarged the territories of the Empire to the East and allowed the energetic Emperor to focus on the Balkans. By 602, a series of successful Byzantine <a href="/wiki/Maurice%27s_Balkan_campaigns" title="Maurice's Balkan campaigns">campaigns</a> had pushed the Avars and Slavs back across the Danube.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Louth_2005_113.E2.80.93115_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Louth_2005_113.E2.80.93115-61">[60]</a></sup> However, Maurice's refusal to ransom several thousand captives taken by the Avars, and his order to the troops to winter in the Danube caused his popularity to plummet. A revolt broke out under an officer named Phocas, who marched the troops back to Constantinople; Maurice and his family were murdered while trying to escape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shrinking_borders">Shrinking borders</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Heraclian_dynasty">Heraclian dynasty</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Heraclian_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Heraclian dynasty">Byzantine Empire under the Heraclian dynasty</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moldovita_murals_2010_16.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2216" data-file-width="3447" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Moldovita_murals_2010_16.jpg/250px-Moldovita_murals_2010_16.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Moldovita_murals_2010_16.jpg/375px-Moldovita_murals_2010_16.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Moldovita_murals_2010_16.jpg/500px-Moldovita_murals_2010_16.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moldovita_murals_2010_16.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(626)" title="Siege of Constantinople (626)">Siege of Constantinople in 626</a> by the combined Avar, Sassanid Persian, and Slavic forces depicted on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Murals" title="Murals">murals</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Moldovi%C8%9Ba_Monastery" title="Moldovița Monastery">Moldovița Monastery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantiumby650AD.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="423" data-file-width="924" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Byzantiumby650AD.svg/300px-Byzantiumby650AD.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Byzantiumby650AD.svg/450px-Byzantiumby650AD.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Byzantiumby650AD.svg/600px-Byzantiumby650AD.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Byzantiumby650AD.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Byzantine Empire in 650 - by this year it had lost all of its southern provinces except the <a href="/wiki/Exarchate_of_Africa" title="Exarchate of Africa">Exarchate of Africa</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After Maurice's murder by <a href="/wiki/Phocas" title="Phocas">Phocas</a>, Khosrau used the pretext to reconquer the <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia_(Roman_province)" title="Mesopotamia (Roman province)">Roman province of Mesopotamia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> Phocas, an unpopular ruler invariably described in Byzantine sources as a "tyrant", was the target of a number of Senate-led plots. He was eventually deposed in 610 by Heraclius, who sailed to Constantinople from <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a> with an icon affixed to the prow of his ship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the accession of Heraclius, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanids" title="Sassanids">Sassanid</a> advance pushed deep into the Levant, occupying <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> and removing the <a href="/wiki/True_Cross" title="True Cross">True Cross</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ctesiphon" title="Ctesiphon">Ctesiphon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> The counter-attack launched by Heraclius took on the character of a holy war, and an <a href="/wiki/Acheiropoieta" title="Acheiropoieta">acheiropoietos</a> image of Christ was carried as a military standard<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> (similarly, when Constantinople was saved from a combined Avar–Sassanid–<a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavic</a> siege in 626, the victory was attributed to the icons of the Virgin that were led in procession by <a href="/wiki/Sergius_I_of_Constantinople" title="Sergius I of Constantinople">Patriarch Sergius</a> about the walls of the city).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> In this very <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(626)" title="Siege of Constantinople (626)">siege of Constantinople of the year 626</a>, amidst the climactic <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Sasanian_War_of_602%E2%80%93628" title="Byzantine–Sasanian War of 602–628">Byzantine–Sasanian War of 602–628</a>, the combined Avar, Sassanid, and Slavic forces unsuccessfully besieged the Byzantine capital between June and July. After this, the Sassanid army was forced to withdraw to <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>. The loss came just after news had reached them of yet another Byzantine victory, where Heraclius's brother <a href="/wiki/Theodore_(brother_of_Heraclius)" title="Theodore (brother of Heraclius)">Theodore</a> scored well against the Persian general <a href="/wiki/Shahin_Vahmanzadegan" title="Shahin Vahmanzadegan">Shahin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich93_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norwich93-68">[67]</a></sup> Following this, Heraclius led an invasion into Sassanid Mesopotamia once again.</p>
<p>The main Sassanid force was destroyed at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Nineveh_(627)" title="Battle of Nineveh (627)">Nineveh</a> in 627, and in 629 Heraclius restored the True Cross to Jerusalem in a majestic ceremony,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup> as he marched into the Sassanid capital of <a href="/wiki/Ctesiphon" title="Ctesiphon">Ctesiphon</a>, where anarchy and civil war reigned as a result of the enduring war. Eventually, the Persians were obliged to withdraw all armed forces and return Sassanid-ruled <a href="/wiki/Egypt_(Roman_province)" title="Egypt (Roman province)">Egypt</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> and whatever imperial territories of Mesopotamia and <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> were in Roman hands at the time of an earlier peace treaty in c. 595. The war had exhausted both the Byzantines and Sassanids, however, and left them extremely vulnerable to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" title="Muslim conquests">Muslim forces</a> that emerged in the following years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> The Byzantines suffered a crushing defeat by the Arabs at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Yarmouk" title="Battle of Yarmouk">Battle of Yarmouk</a> in 636, while <a href="/wiki/Ctesiphon" title="Ctesiphon">Ctesiphon</a> fell in 637.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Siege_of_Constantinople_.28674.E2.80.93678.29">Siege of Constantinople (674–678)</span></h4>
<p>The Arabs, now firmly in <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_the_Levant" title="Muslim conquest of the Levant">control of Syria and the Levant</a>, sent frequent raiding parties deep into Asia Minor, and in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(674%E2%80%9378)" title="Siege of Constantinople (674–78)">674–678 laid siege to Constantinople</a> itself. The Arab fleet was finally repulsed through the use of <a href="/wiki/Greek_fire" title="Greek fire">Greek fire</a>, and a thirty-years' truce was signed between the Empire and the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> However, the <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolian</a> raids continued unabated, and accelerated the demise of classical urban culture, with the inhabitants of many cities either refortifying much smaller areas within the old city walls, or relocating entirely to nearby fortresses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> Constantinople itself dropped substantially in size, from 500,000 inhabitants to just 40,000–70,000, and, like other urban centres, it was partly ruralised. The city also lost the free grain shipments in 618, after Egypt fell first to the Persians and then to the Arabs, and public wheat distribution ceased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>The void left by the disappearance of the old semi-autonomous civic institutions was filled by the theme system, which entailed dividing Asia Minor into "provinces" occupied by distinct armies that assumed civil authority and answered directly to the imperial administration. This system may have had its roots in certain <i>ad hoc</i> measures taken by Heraclius, but over the course of the 7th century it developed into an entirely new system of imperial governance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> The massive cultural and institutional restructuring of the Empire consequent on the loss of territory in the 7th century has been said to have caused a decisive break in east Mediterranean <i>Romanness</i> and that the Byzantine state is subsequently best understood as another successor state rather than a real continuation of the Roman Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greekfire-madridskylitzes1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1543" data-file-width="3013" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Greekfire-madridskylitzes1.jpg/250px-Greekfire-madridskylitzes1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Greekfire-madridskylitzes1.jpg/375px-Greekfire-madridskylitzes1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Greekfire-madridskylitzes1.jpg/500px-Greekfire-madridskylitzes1.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greekfire-madridskylitzes1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Greek_fire" title="Greek fire">Greek fire</a> was first used by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_Navy" title="Byzantine Navy">Byzantine Navy</a> during the Byzantine–Arab Wars (from the <a href="/wiki/Madrid_Skylitzes" title="Madrid Skylitzes">Madrid Skylitzes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biblioteca_Nacional_de_Espa%C3%B1a" title="Biblioteca Nacional de España">Biblioteca Nacional de España</a>, Madrid).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The withdrawal of large numbers of troops from the Balkans to combat the Persians and then the Arabs in the east opened the door for the gradual southward expansion of Slavic peoples into the peninsula, and, as in Asia Minor, many cities shrank to small fortified settlements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> In the 670s, the <a href="/wiki/Bulgars" title="Bulgars">Bulgars</a> were pushed south of the Danube by the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Khazars" title="Khazars">Khazars</a>. In 680, Byzantine forces sent to disperse these new settlements were defeated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haldon_1990_66.E2.80.9367_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haldon_1990_66.E2.80.9367-78">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 681, <a href="/wiki/Constantine_IV" title="Constantine IV">Constantine IV</a> signed a treaty with the Bulgar khan <a href="/wiki/Asparukh_of_Bulgaria" title="Asparukh of Bulgaria">Asparukh</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/First_Bulgarian_Empire" title="First Bulgarian Empire">new Bulgarian state</a> assumed sovereignty over a number of Slavic tribes that had previously, at least in name, recognised Byzantine rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haldon_1990_66.E2.80.9367_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Haldon_1990_66.E2.80.9367-78">[77]</a></sup> In 687–688, the final Heraclian emperor, <a href="/wiki/Justinian_II" title="Justinian II">Justinian II</a>, led an expedition against the Slavs and Bulgarians, and made significant gains, although the fact that he had to fight his way from <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a> to <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(region)" title="Macedonia (region)">Macedonia</a> demonstrates the degree to which Byzantine power in the north Balkans had declined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>Justinian II attempted to break the power of the urban aristocracy through severe taxation and the appointment of "outsiders" to administrative posts. He was driven from power in 695, and took shelter first with the Khazars and then with the Bulgarians. In 705, he returned to Constantinople with the armies of the <a href="/wiki/Bulgarians" title="Bulgarians">Bulgarian</a> khan <a href="/wiki/Tervel_of_Bulgaria" title="Tervel of Bulgaria">Tervel</a>, retook the throne, and instituted a reign of terror against his enemies. With his final overthrow in 711, supported once more by the urban aristocracy, the Heraclian dynasty came to an end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Isaurian_dynasty_to_the_accession_of_Basil_I">Isaurian dynasty to the accession of Basil I</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Isaurian_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Isaurian dynasty">Byzantine Empire under the Isaurian dynasty</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ByzantineEmpire717%2Bextrainfo%2Bthemes.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="423" data-file-width="925" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/ByzantineEmpire717%2Bextrainfo%2Bthemes.svg/300px-ByzantineEmpire717%2Bextrainfo%2Bthemes.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/ByzantineEmpire717%2Bextrainfo%2Bthemes.svg/450px-ByzantineEmpire717%2Bextrainfo%2Bthemes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/ByzantineEmpire717%2Bextrainfo%2Bthemes.svg/600px-ByzantineEmpire717%2Bextrainfo%2Bthemes.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ByzantineEmpire717%2Bextrainfo%2Bthemes.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Byzantine Empire at the accession of Leo III, c. 717. Striped area indicates land raided by the Arabs.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Leo_III_the_Isaurian" title="Leo III the Isaurian">Leo III the Isaurian</a> turned back the Muslim assault in 718 and addressed himself to the task of reorganising and consolidating the themes in Asia Minor. His successor, <a href="/wiki/Constantine_V" title="Constantine V">Constantine V</a>, won noteworthy victories in northern Syria and thoroughly undermined Bulgarian strength.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Taking advantage of the Empire's weakness after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Revolt_of_Thomas_the_Slav" title="Revolt of Thomas the Slav">Revolt of Thomas the Slav</a> in the early 820s, the Arabs re-emerged and <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Crete" title="Emirate of Crete">captured Crete</a>. They also successfully attacked Sicily, but in 863 general <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petronas_the_Patrician" title="Petronas the Patrician">Petronas</a> gained a <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lalakaon" title="Battle of Lalakaon">decisive victory</a> against <a href="/wiki/Umar_al-Aqta" title="Umar al-Aqta">Umar al-Aqta</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Emir" title="Emir">emir</a> of Melitene (<a href="/wiki/Malatya" title="Malatya">Malatya</a>). Under the leadership of emperor <a href="/wiki/Krum" title="Krum">Krum</a>, the Bulgarian threat also re-emerged, but in 815–816 Krum's son, <a href="/wiki/Omurtag_of_Bulgaria" title="Omurtag of Bulgaria">Omurtag</a>, signed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_815" title="Treaty of 815">peace treaty</a> with <a href="/wiki/Leo_V_the_Armenian" title="Leo V the Armenian">Leo V</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_dispute_over_iconoclasm">Religious dispute over iconoclasm</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_iconoclasm" title="Byzantine iconoclasm">Byzantine iconoclasm</a></div>
<p>The 8th and early 9th centuries were also dominated by controversy and religious division over <a href="/wiki/Iconoclasm" title="Iconoclasm">Iconoclasm</a>, which was the main political issue in the Empire for over a century. <a href="/wiki/Icon" title="Icon">Icons</a> (here meaning all forms of religious imagery) were banned by Leo and Constantine from around 730, leading to revolts by <a href="/wiki/Iconodule" title="Iconodule">iconodules</a> (supporters of icons) throughout the empire. After the efforts of empress <a href="/wiki/Irene_of_Athens" title="Irene of Athens">Irene</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Nicaea" title="Second Council of Nicaea">Second Council of Nicaea</a> met in 787 and affirmed that icons could be venerated but not worshiped. Irene is said to have endeavoured to negotiate a marriage between herself and Charlemagne, but, according to <a href="/wiki/Theophanes_the_Confessor" title="Theophanes the Confessor">Theophanes the Confessor</a>, the scheme was frustrated by Aetios, one of her favourites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G89_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-G89-83">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the early 9th century, Leo V reintroduced the policy of iconoclasm, but in 843 empress <a href="/wiki/Theodora_(wife_of_Theophilos)" title="Theodora (wife of Theophilos)">Theodora</a> restored the veneration of icons with the help of <a href="/wiki/Methodios_I_of_Constantinople" title="Methodios I of Constantinople">Patriarch Methodios</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P11_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-P11-84">[83]</a></sup> Iconoclasm played a part in the further alienation of East from West, which worsened during the so-called <a href="/wiki/Photian_schism" title="Photian schism">Photian schism</a>, when <a href="/wiki/Pope_Nicholas_I" title="Pope Nicholas I">Pope Nicholas I</a> challenged the elevation of <a href="/wiki/Photios_I_of_Constantinople" title="Photios I of Constantinople">Photios</a> to the patriarchate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Macedonian_dynasty_and_resurgence_.28867.E2.80.931025.29">Macedonian dynasty and resurgence (867–1025)</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Macedonian_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Macedonian dynasty">Byzantine Empire under the Macedonian dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ByzantineEmpire867AD4lightpurple.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="370" data-file-width="800" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/ByzantineEmpire867AD4lightpurple.PNG/300px-ByzantineEmpire867AD4lightpurple.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/ByzantineEmpire867AD4lightpurple.PNG/450px-ByzantineEmpire867AD4lightpurple.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/ByzantineEmpire867AD4lightpurple.PNG/600px-ByzantineEmpire867AD4lightpurple.PNG 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ByzantineEmpire867AD4lightpurple.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Byzantine Empire, c. 867.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The accession of <a href="/wiki/Basil_I" title="Basil I">Basil I</a> to the throne in 867 marks the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_dynasty" title="Macedonian dynasty">Macedonian dynasty</a>, which would rule for the next two and a half centuries. This dynasty included some of the most able emperors in Byzantium's history, and the period is one of revival and resurgence. The Empire moved from defending against external enemies to reconquest of territories formerly lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-95_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-95-86">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to a reassertion of Byzantine military power and political authority, the period under the Macedonian dynasty is characterised by a cultural revival in spheres such as philosophy and the arts. There was a conscious effort to restore the brilliance of the period before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavic_invasion_of_the_Balkans" title="Slavic invasion of the Balkans">Slavic</a> and subsequent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" title="Muslim conquests">Arab invasions</a>, and the Macedonian era has been dubbed the "Golden Age" of Byzantium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-95_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-95-86">[85]</a></sup> Though the Empire was significantly smaller than during the reign of Justinian, it had regained significant strength, as the remaining territories were less geographically dispersed and more politically, economically, and culturally integrated.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wars_against_the_Arabs">Wars against the Arabs</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Arab_Wars_(780%E2%80%931180)" title="Byzantine–Arab Wars (780–1180)">Byzantine–Arab Wars (780–1180)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leo_Phokas_defeats_Hambdan_at_Adrassos.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="581" data-file-width="1304" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Leo_Phokas_defeats_Hambdan_at_Adrassos.png/250px-Leo_Phokas_defeats_Hambdan_at_Adrassos.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Leo_Phokas_defeats_Hambdan_at_Adrassos.png/375px-Leo_Phokas_defeats_Hambdan_at_Adrassos.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Leo_Phokas_defeats_Hambdan_at_Adrassos.png/500px-Leo_Phokas_defeats_Hambdan_at_Adrassos.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leo_Phokas_defeats_Hambdan_at_Adrassos.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The general <a href="/wiki/Leo_Phokas_the_Younger" title="Leo Phokas the Younger">Leo Phokas</a> defeats the <a href="/wiki/Hamdanid_dynasty" title="Hamdanid dynasty">Hamdanid</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Aleppo" title="Emirate of Aleppo">Emirate of Aleppo</a> at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Andrassos" title="Battle of Andrassos">Andrassos</a> in 960, from the <i><a href="/wiki/Madrid_Skylitzes" title="Madrid Skylitzes">Madrid Skylitzes</a></i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the early years of Basil I's reign, Arab raids on the coasts of Dalmatia were successfully repelled, and the region once again came under secure Byzantine control. This enabled Byzantine missionaries to penetrate to the interior and convert the Serbs and the principalities of modern-day <a href="/wiki/Herzegovina" title="Herzegovina">Herzegovina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a> to Orthodox Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-96_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-96-87">[86]</a></sup> An attempt to retake <a href="/wiki/Malta_(island)" title="Malta (island)">Malta</a> ended disastrously, however, when the local population sided with the Arabs and massacred the Byzantine garrison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karlin-Heyer_1967_24_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karlin-Heyer_1967_24-88">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>By contrast, the Byzantine position in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Italy" title="Southern Italy">Southern Italy</a> was gradually consolidated so that by 873 <a href="/wiki/Bari" title="Bari">Bari</a> had once again come under Byzantine rule,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-96_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-96-87">[86]</a></sup> and most of Southern Italy would remain in the Empire for the next 200 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karlin-Heyer_1967_24_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Karlin-Heyer_1967_24-88">[87]</a></sup> On the more important eastern front, the Empire rebuilt its defences and went on the offensive. The <a href="/wiki/Paulicianism" title="Paulicianism">Paulicians</a> were defeated and their capital of Tephrike (Divrigi) taken, while the offensive against the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a> began with the recapture of <a href="/wiki/Samosata" title="Samosata">Samosata</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-96_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-96-87">[86]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol1v.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="458" data-file-width="412" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol1v.jpg/200px-Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol1v.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol1v.jpg/300px-Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol1v.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol1v.jpg/400px-Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol1v.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol1v.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The military successes of the 10th century were coupled with a major cultural revival, the so-called <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_Renaissance" title="Macedonian Renaissance">Macedonian Renaissance</a>. Miniature from the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Psalter" title="Paris Psalter">Paris Psalter</a>, an example of Hellenistic-influenced art.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Under Basil's son and successor, <a href="/wiki/Leo_VI_the_Wise" title="Leo VI the Wise">Leo VI the Wise</a>, the gains in the east against the now-weak Abbasid Caliphate continued. However, Sicily was lost to the Arabs in 902, and in 904 <a href="/wiki/Thessaloniki" title="Thessaloniki">Thessaloniki</a>, the Empire's second city, was sacked by an Arab fleet. The weakness of the Empire in the naval sphere was quickly rectified, so that a few years later a Byzantine fleet had re-occupied <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, lost in the 7th century, and also stormed <a href="/wiki/Latakia" title="Latakia">Laodicea</a> in Syria. Despite this revenge, the Byzantines were still unable to strike a decisive blow against the Muslims, who inflicted a crushing defeat on the imperial forces when they attempted to regain <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Crete" title="Emirate of Crete">Crete</a> in 911.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B101_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-B101-89">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>The death of the Bulgarian tsar <a href="/wiki/Simeon_I_of_Bulgaria" title="Simeon I of Bulgaria">Simeon I</a> in 927 severely weakened the Bulgarians, allowing the Byzantines to concentrate on the eastern front.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-107_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-107-90">[89]</a></sup> Melitene was permanently recaptured in 934, and in 943 the famous general <a href="/wiki/John_Kourkouas" title="John Kourkouas">John Kourkouas</a> continued the offensive in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> with some noteworthy victories, culminating in the reconquest of <a href="/wiki/Edessa" title="Edessa">Edessa</a>. Kourkouas was especially celebrated for returning to Constantinople the venerated <a href="/wiki/Image_of_Edessa" title="Image of Edessa">Mandylion</a>, a relic purportedly imprinted with a portrait of Christ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-108_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-108-91">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>The soldier-emperors <a href="/wiki/Nikephoros_II_Phokas" title="Nikephoros II Phokas">Nikephoros II Phokas</a> (reigned 963–969) and <a href="/wiki/John_I_Tzimiskes" title="John I Tzimiskes">John I Tzimiskes</a> (969–976) expanded the empire well into Syria, defeating the emirs of north-west <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>. The great city of <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a> was taken by Nikephoros in 962, and the Arabs were decisively expelled from Crete in 963. The recapture of Crete put an end to Arab raids in the Aegean, allowing mainland Greece to flourish once again. <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> was permanently retaken in 965, and the successes of Nikephoros culminated in 969 with the recapture of <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a>, which he incorporated as a province of the Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-112_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-112-92">[91]</a></sup> His successor John Tzimiskes recaptured Damascus, <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acre,_Israel" title="Acre, Israel">Acre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">Sidon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caesarea" title="Caesarea">Caesarea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tiberias" title="Tiberias">Tiberias</a>, putting Byzantine armies within striking distance of Jerusalem, although the Muslim power centres in Iraq and Egypt were left untouched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-113_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-113-93">[92]</a></sup> After much campaigning in the north, the last Arab threat to Byzantium, the rich province of Sicily, was targeted in 1025 by <a href="/wiki/Basil_II" title="Basil II">Basil II</a>, who died before the expedition could be completed. Nevertheless, by that time the Empire stretched from the straits of <a href="/wiki/Messina" title="Messina">Messina</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> and from the Danube to Syria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-116_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-116-94">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wars_against_the_Bulgarian_Empire">Wars against the Bulgarian Empire</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Bulgarian_wars" title="Byzantine–Bulgarian wars">Byzantine–Bulgarian wars</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Basilios_II.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="389" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Basilios_II.jpg/200px-Basilios_II.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Basilios_II.jpg/300px-Basilios_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/dc/Basilios_II.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Basilios_II.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Emperor Basil II (r. 976–1025)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The traditional struggle with the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">See of Rome</a> continued through the Macedonian period, spurred by the question of religious supremacy over the <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_Bulgaria" title="Christianization of Bulgaria">newly Christianised</a> state of <a href="/wiki/First_Bulgarian_Empire" title="First Bulgarian Empire">Bulgaria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-95_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-95-86">[85]</a></sup> Ending eighty years of peace between the two states, the powerful Bulgarian tsar <a href="/wiki/Simeon_I_of_Bulgaria" title="Simeon I of Bulgaria">Simeon I</a> invaded in 894 but was pushed back by the Byzantines, who used their fleet to sail up the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> to attack the Bulgarian rear, enlisting the support of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hungarian_people" title="Hungarian people">Hungarians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> The Byzantines were defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Boulgarophygon" title="Battle of Boulgarophygon">Battle of Boulgarophygon</a> in 896, however, and agreed to pay annual subsidies to the Bulgarians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B101_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-B101-89">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>Leo the Wise died in 912, and hostilities soon resumed as Simeon marched to Constantinople at the head of a large army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup> Though the walls of the city were impregnable, the Byzantine administration was in disarray and Simeon was invited into the city, where he was granted the crown of <i>basileus</i> (emperor) of Bulgaria and had the young emperor <a href="/wiki/Constantine_VII" title="Constantine VII">Constantine VII</a> marry one of his daughters.
