When a revolt in Constantinople halted his dynastic project, he again invaded Thrace and conquered <a href="/wiki/Edirne" title="Edirne">Adrianople</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> The Empire now faced the problem of a powerful Christian state within a few days' marching distance from Constantinople,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-95_86-3"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-95-86">[85]</a></sup> as well as having to fight on two fronts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B101_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-B101-89">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>A great imperial expedition under <a href="/wiki/Leo_Phokas_the_Elder" title="Leo Phokas the Elder">Leo Phocas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Romanos_I_Lekapenos" title="Romanos I Lekapenos">Romanos I Lekapenos</a> ended with another crushing Byzantine defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Achelous_(917)" title="Battle of Achelous (917)">Battle of Achelous</a> in 917, and the following year the Bulgarians were free to ravage northern Greece. Adrianople was plundered again in 923, and a Bulgarian army laid siege to Constantinople in 924. Simeon died suddenly in 927, however, and Bulgarian power collapsed with him. Bulgaria and Byzantium entered a long period of peaceful relations, and the Empire was now free to concentrate on the eastern front against the Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup> In 968, Bulgaria was overrun by the <a href="/wiki/Rus%27_people" title="Rus' people">Rus'</a> under <a href="/wiki/Sviatoslav_I_of_Kiev" title="Sviatoslav I of Kiev">Sviatoslav I of Kiev</a>, but three years later, John I Tzimiskes <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Dorostolon" title="Siege of Dorostolon">defeated</a> the Rus' and re-incorporated Eastern Bulgaria into the Byzantine Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_Byzantine_Empire_1025-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1104" data-file-width="1963" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Map_Byzantine_Empire_1025-en.svg/250px-Map_Byzantine_Empire_1025-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Map_Byzantine_Empire_1025-en.svg/375px-Map_Byzantine_Empire_1025-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Map_Byzantine_Empire_1025-en.svg/500px-Map_Byzantine_Empire_1025-en.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_Byzantine_Empire_1025-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The extent of the Empire under <a href="/wiki/Basil_II" title="Basil II">Basil II</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Bulgarian resistance revived under the rule of the <a href="/wiki/Cometopuli_dynasty" title="Cometopuli dynasty">Cometopuli dynasty</a>, but the new emperor Basil II (r. 976–1025) made the submission of the Bulgarians his primary goal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B115_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-B115-100">[99]</a></sup> Basil's first expedition against Bulgaria, however, resulted in a humiliating defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Gates_of_Trajan" title="Battle of the Gates of Trajan">Gates of Trajan</a>. For the next few years, the emperor would be preoccupied with internal revolts in Anatolia, while the Bulgarians expanded their realm in the Balkans. The war dragged on for nearly twenty years. The Byzantine victories of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Spercheios" title="Battle of Spercheios">Spercheios</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Skopje" title="Battle of Skopje">Skopje</a> decisively weakened the Bulgarian army, and in annual campaigns, Basil methodically reduced the Bulgarian strongholds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B115_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-B115-100">[99]</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kleidion" title="Battle of Kleidion">Battle of Kleidion</a> in 1014 the Bulgarians were annihilated: their army was captured, and it is said that 99 out of every 100 men were blinded, with the hundredth man left with one eye so he could lead his compatriots home. When Tsar <a href="/wiki/Samuel_of_Bulgaria" title="Samuel of Bulgaria">Samuil</a> saw the broken remains of his once formidable army, he died of shock. By 1018, the last Bulgarian strongholds had surrendered, and the country became part of the Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B115_100-2"><a href="#cite_note-B115-100">[99]</a></sup> This victory restored the Danube frontier, which had not been held since the days of the emperor Heraclius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-116_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-116-94">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_with_the_Kievan_Rus.27">Relations with the Kievan Rus'</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A6%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%8C%D0%B3%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="435" data-file-width="640" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/%D0%A6%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%8C%D0%B3%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B4.jpg/250px-%D0%A6%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%8C%D0%B3%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/%D0%A6%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%8C%D0%B3%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B4.jpg/375px-%D0%A6%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%8C%D0%B3%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/%D0%A6%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%8C%D0%B3%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B4.jpg/500px-%D0%A6%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%8C%D0%B3%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B4.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A6%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%8C%D0%B3%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Rus%27_people" title="Rus' people">Rus'</a> under the walls of Constantinople (860)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Between 850 and 1100, the Empire developed a mixed relationship with the new state of the <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus'</a>, which had emerged to the north across the Black Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-114-115_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-114-115-101">[100]</a></sup> This relationship would have long-lasting repercussions in the history of the <a href="/wiki/East_Slavs" title="East Slavs">East Slavs</a>, and the Empire quickly became the main <a href="/wiki/Trade_route_from_the_Varangians_to_the_Greeks" title="Trade route from the Varangians to the Greeks">trading</a> and cultural partner for Kiev. The Rus' launched their first attack against Constantinople <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rus%27%E2%80%93Byzantine_War_(860)" title="Rus'–Byzantine War (860)">in 860</a>, pillaging the suburbs of the city. In 941, <a href="/wiki/Rus%27%E2%80%93Byzantine_War_(941)" title="Rus'–Byzantine War (941)">they appeared on the Asian shore</a> of the Bosphorus, but this time they were crushed, an indication of the improvements in the Byzantine military position after 907, when <a href="/wiki/Rus%27%E2%80%93Byzantine_Treaty_(907)" title="Rus'–Byzantine Treaty (907)">only diplomacy had been able to push back the invaders</a>. Basil II could not ignore the emerging power of the Rus', and, following the example of his predecessors, he used religion as a means for the achievement of political purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cameron_2009_77_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cameron_2009_77-102">[101]</a></sup> Rus'–Byzantine relations became closer following the marriage of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anna_Porphyrogeneta" title="Anna Porphyrogeneta">Anna Porphyrogeneta</a> to <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_the_Great" title="Vladimir the Great">Vladimir the Great</a> in 988, and the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_Kievan_Rus%27" title="Christianization of Kievan Rus'">Christianisation of the Rus'</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-114-115_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-114-115-101">[100]</a></sup> Byzantine priests, architects, and artists were invited to work on numerous cathedrals and churches around Rus', expanding Byzantine cultural influence even further, while numerous Rus' served in the Byzantine army as mercenaries, most notably as the famous <a href="/wiki/Varangian_Guard" title="Varangian Guard">Varangian Guard</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-114-115_101-2"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-114-115-101">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>Even after the Christianisation of the Rus', however, relations were not always friendly. The most serious conflict between the two powers was the war of 968–971 in Bulgaria, but several Rus' raiding expeditions against the Byzantine cities of the Black Sea coast and Constantinople itself are also recorded. Although most were repulsed, they were often followed by treaties that were generally favourable to the Rus', such as the one concluded at the end of <a href="/wiki/Rus%27%E2%80%93Byzantine_War_(1043)" title="Rus'–Byzantine War (1043)">the war of 1043</a>, during which the Rus' gave an indication of their ambitions to compete with the Byzantines as an independent power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cameron_2009_77_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cameron_2009_77-102">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Apex">Apex</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bizansist_touchup.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="970" data-file-width="2516" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Bizansist_touchup.jpg/250px-Bizansist_touchup.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Bizansist_touchup.jpg/375px-Bizansist_touchup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Bizansist_touchup.jpg/500px-Bizansist_touchup.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bizansist_touchup.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Constantinople became the largest and wealthiest city in Europe between the 9th and 11th centuries</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By 1025, the date of Basil II's death, the Byzantine Empire stretched from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Armenia_(Middle_Ages)" title="Kingdom of Armenia (Middle Ages)">Armenia</a> in the east to <a href="/wiki/Calabria" title="Calabria">Calabria</a> in Southern Italy in the west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-116_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-116-94">[93]</a></sup> Many successes had been achieved, ranging from the conquest of Bulgaria to the annexation of parts of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Georgia" title="Kingdom of Georgia">Georgia</a> and Armenia, and the reconquest of Crete, Cyprus, and the important city of Antioch. These were not temporary tactical gains but long-term reconquests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-96_87-3"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-96-87">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Leo VI achieved the complete codification of Byzantine law in Greek. This monumental work of 60 volumes became the foundation of all subsequent Byzantine law and is still studied today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-97-98_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-97-98-103">[102]</a></sup> Leo also reformed the administration of the Empire, redrawing the borders of the administrative subdivisions (the <i><a href="/wiki/Theme_(Byzantine_district)" title="Theme (Byzantine district)">Themata</a></i>, or "Themes") and tidying up the system of ranks and privileges, as well as regulating the behaviour of the various trade guilds in Constantinople. Leo's reform did much to reduce the previous fragmentation of the Empire, which henceforth had one center of power, Constantinople.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-98-99_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-98-99-104">[103]</a></sup> However, the increasing military success of the Empire greatly enriched and empowered the provincial nobility with respect to the peasantry, who were essentially reduced to a state of serfdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-98-109_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-98-109-105">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under the Macedonian emperors, the city of Constantinople flourished, becoming the largest and wealthiest city in Europe, with a population of approximately 400,000 in the 9th and 10th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> During this period, the Byzantine Empire employed a strong civil service staffed by competent aristocrats that oversaw the collection of taxes, domestic administration, and foreign policy. The Macedonian emperors also increased the Empire's wealth by fostering trade with Western Europe, particularly through <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_silk" title="Byzantine silk">the sale of silk</a> and metalwork.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Split_between_Orthodox_Christianity_and_Catholicism_.281054.29">Split between Orthodox Christianity and Catholicism (1054)</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East–West Schism">East–West Schism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cyril-methodius-small.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="377" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Cyril-methodius-small.jpg/200px-Cyril-methodius-small.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Cyril-methodius-small.jpg/300px-Cyril-methodius-small.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c6/Cyril-methodius-small.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cyril-methodius-small.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mural of <a href="/wiki/Saints_Cyril_and_Methodius" title="Saints Cyril and Methodius">Saints Cyril and Methodius</a>, 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Troyan_Monastery" title="Troyan Monastery">Troyan Monastery</a>, Bulgaria</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedonian_period" title="Macedonian period">Macedonian period</a> also included events of momentous religious significance. The conversion of the Bulgarians, Serbs and <a href="/wiki/Rus%27_people" title="Rus' people">Rus'</a> to Orthodox Christianity permanently changed the religious map of Europe and still resonates today. <a href="/wiki/Saints_Cyril_and_Methodius" title="Saints Cyril and Methodius">Cyril and Methodius</a>, two Byzantine Greek brothers from Thessaloniki, contributed significantly to the <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_the_Slavs" title="Christianization of the Slavs">Christianization of the Slavs</a> and in the process devised the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glagolitic_alphabet" title="Glagolitic alphabet">Glagolitic alphabet</a>, ancestor to the <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_script" title="Cyrillic script">Cyrillic script</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1054, relations between the Eastern and Western traditions within the Christian Church reached a terminal crisis, known as the <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East–West Schism">East–West Schism</a>. Although there was a formal declaration of institutional separation, on July 16, when three papal legates entered the Hagia Sophia during Divine Liturgy on a Saturday afternoon and placed a bull of excommunication on the altar,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup> the so-called Great Schism was actually the culmination of centuries of gradual separation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crisis_and_fragmentation">Crisis and fragmentation</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Empire" title="Empire">Empire</a> soon fell into a period of difficulties, caused to a large extent by the undermining of the theme system and the neglect of the military. Nikephoros II, John Tzimiskes, and Basil II changed the military divisions (<span lang="grc">τάγματα</span>, <i><a href="/wiki/Tagma_(military)" title="Tagma (military)">tagmata</a></i>) from a rapid response, primarily defensive, citizen army into a professional, campaigning army, increasingly manned by <a href="/wiki/Mercenary" title="Mercenary">mercenaries</a>. Mercenaries were expensive, however, and as the threat of invasion receded in the 10th century, so did the need for maintaining large garrisons and expensive fortifications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> Basil II left a burgeoning treasury upon his death, but he neglected to plan for his succession. None of his immediate successors had any particular military or political talent and the administration of the Empire increasingly fell into the hands of the civil service. Efforts to revive the Byzantine economy only resulted in <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">inflation</a> and a debased gold coinage. The army was now seen as both an unnecessary expense and a political threat. Native troops were therefore cashiered and replaced by foreign mercenaries on specific contract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PM_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-PM-112">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the same time, the <a href="/wiki/Empire" title="Empire">Empire</a> was faced with new enemies. Provinces in southern Italy faced the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italo-Normans" title="Italo-Normans">Normans</a>, who arrived in Italy at the beginning of the 11th century. During a period of strife between Constantinople and Rome culminating in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East-West_Schism" title="East-West Schism">East-West Schism</a> of 1054, the Normans began to advance, slowly but steadily, into Byzantine Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Reggio_Calabria" title="Reggio Calabria">Reggio</a>, the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Tagma_(military)" title="Tagma (military)">tagma</a> of Calabria, was captured in 1060 by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Guiscard" title="Robert Guiscard">Robert Guiscard</a>, followed by <a href="/wiki/Otranto" title="Otranto">Otranto</a> in 1068. Bari, the main Byzantine stronghold in Apulia, was besieged in August 1068 and <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Bari" title="Siege of Bari">fell in April 1071</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> The Byzantines also lost their influence over the <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia" title="Dalmatia">Dalmatian</a> coastal cities to <a href="/wiki/Peter_Kre%C5%A1imir_IV_of_Croatia" title="Peter Krešimir IV of Croatia">Peter Krešimir IV of Croatia</a> (r. 1058–1074/1075) in 1069.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_seizure_of_Edessa_in_Syria_by_the_Byzantine_army_and_the_Arabic_counterattack_from_the_Chronicle_of_John_Skylitzes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="705" data-file-width="1709" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/The_seizure_of_Edessa_in_Syria_by_the_Byzantine_army_and_the_Arabic_counterattack_from_the_Chronicle_of_John_Skylitzes.jpg/250px-The_seizure_of_Edessa_in_Syria_by_the_Byzantine_army_and_the_Arabic_counterattack_from_the_Chronicle_of_John_Skylitzes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/The_seizure_of_Edessa_in_Syria_by_the_Byzantine_army_and_the_Arabic_counterattack_from_the_Chronicle_of_John_Skylitzes.jpg/375px-The_seizure_of_Edessa_in_Syria_by_the_Byzantine_army_and_the_Arabic_counterattack_from_the_Chronicle_of_John_Skylitzes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/The_seizure_of_Edessa_in_Syria_by_the_Byzantine_army_and_the_Arabic_counterattack_from_the_Chronicle_of_John_Skylitzes.jpg/500px-The_seizure_of_Edessa_in_Syria_by_the_Byzantine_army_and_the_Arabic_counterattack_from_the_Chronicle_of_John_Skylitzes.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_seizure_of_Edessa_in_Syria_by_the_Byzantine_army_and_the_Arabic_counterattack_from_the_Chronicle_of_John_Skylitzes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The seizure of <a href="/wiki/Edessa" title="Edessa">Edessa</a> (1031) by the Byzantines under <a href="/wiki/George_Maniakes" title="George Maniakes">George Maniakes</a> and the counterattack by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seljuk_Turks" title="Seljuk Turks">Seljuk Turks</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The greatest disaster took place in Asia Minor, however, where the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seljuq_Turks" title="Seljuq Turks">Seljuq Turks</a> made their first explorations across the Byzantine frontier into Armenia in 1065 and 1067. The emergency lent weight to the military aristocracy in Anatolia, who in 1068 secured the election of one of their own, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanos_IV" title="Romanos IV">Romanos Diogenes</a>, as emperor. In the summer of 1071, Romanos undertook a massive eastern campaign to draw the Seljuks into a general engagement with the Byzantine army. At the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Manzikert" title="Battle of Manzikert">Battle of Manzikert</a>, Romanos suffered a surprise defeat by <a href="/wiki/Sultan" title="Sultan">Sultan</a> <a href="/wiki/Alp_Arslan" title="Alp Arslan">Alp Arslan</a>, and he was captured. Alp Arslan treated him with respect and imposed no harsh terms on the Byzantines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PM_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-PM-112">[111]</a></sup> In Constantinople, however, a coup put in power <a href="/wiki/Michael_VII_Doukas" title="Michael VII Doukas">Michael Doukas</a>, who soon faced the opposition of <a href="/wiki/Nikephoros_Bryennios_the_Elder" title="Nikephoros Bryennios the Elder">Nikephoros Bryennios</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nikephoros_III" title="Nikephoros III">Nikephoros Botaneiates</a>. By 1081, the Seljuks had expanded their rule over virtually the entire Anatolian plateau from Armenia in the east to <a href="/wiki/Bithynia" title="Bithynia">Bithynia</a> in the west, and they had founded their capital at <a href="/wiki/Nicaea" title="Nicaea">Nicaea</a>, just 90 kilometres (56 miles) from Constantinople.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Komnenian_dynasty_and_the_crusaders">Komnenian dynasty and the crusaders</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Komnenos_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Komnenos dynasty">Byzantine Empire under the Komnenos dynasty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Komnenian_restoration" title="Komnenian restoration">Komnenian restoration</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexios_I_Komnenos.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="409" data-file-width="291" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Alexios_I_Komnenos.jpg/200px-Alexios_I_Komnenos.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8c/Alexios_I_Komnenos.jpg 1.5x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexios_I_Komnenos.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Alexios_I_Komnenos" title="Alexios I Komnenos">Alexios I</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Komnenos" title="Komnenos">Komnenos dynasty</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the Komnenian, or Comnenian, period from about 1081 to about 1185, the five emperors of the <a href="/wiki/Komnenos" title="Komnenos">Komnenos dynasty</a> (Alexios I, John II, Manuel I, Alexios II, and Andronikos I) presided over a sustained, though ultimately incomplete, restoration of the military, territorial, economic, and political position of the Byzantine Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-190_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-190-117">[116]</a></sup> Although the Seljuk Turks occupied the heartland of the Empire in Anatolia, most Byzantine military efforts during this period were directed against Western powers, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Normans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-190_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-190-117">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Empire under the Komnenoi played a key role in the history of the Crusades in the Holy Land, which Alexios I had helped bring about, while also exerting enormous cultural and political influence in Europe, the Near East, and the lands around the Mediterranean Sea under John and Manuel. Contact between Byzantium and the "Latin" West, including the Crusader states, increased significantly during the Komnenian period. Venetian and other Italian traders became resident in large numbers in Constantinople and the empire (there were an estimated 60,000 Latins in Constantinople alone, out of a population of three to four hundred thousand), and their presence together with the numerous Latin mercenaries who were employed by Manuel helped to spread Byzantine technology, art, literature and culture throughout the Latin West, while also leading to a flow of Western ideas and customs into the Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>In terms of prosperity and cultural life, the Komnenian period was one of the peaks in Byzantine history,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup> and Constantinople remained the leading city of the Christian world in size, wealth, and culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup> There was a renewed interest in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_Greek_philosophy" title="Classical Greek philosophy">classical Greek philosophy</a>, as well as an increase in literary output in vernacular Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-190-218_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-190-218-121">[120]</a></sup> Byzantine art and literature held a pre-eminent place in Europe, and the cultural impact of Byzantine art on the west during this period was enormous and of long lasting significance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-218_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-218-122">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alexios_I_and_the_First_Crusade">Alexios I and the First Crusade</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Alexios_I_Komnenos" title="Alexios I Komnenos">Alexios I Komnenos</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_Macedonia_1045CE.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="335" data-file-width="423" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Byzantine_Macedonia_1045CE.svg/220px-Byzantine_Macedonia_1045CE.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Byzantine_Macedonia_1045CE.svg/330px-Byzantine_Macedonia_1045CE.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Byzantine_Macedonia_1045CE.svg/440px-Byzantine_Macedonia_1045CE.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_Macedonia_1045CE.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Province (doukaton) of the Byzantine Empire ca. 1045.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantiumforecrusades.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="550" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Byzantiumforecrusades.jpg/220px-Byzantiumforecrusades.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Byzantiumforecrusades.jpg/330px-Byzantiumforecrusades.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Byzantiumforecrusades.jpg/440px-Byzantiumforecrusades.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Byzantiumforecrusades.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Byzantine Empire and the Seljuk <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_R%C3%BBm" title="Sultanate of Rûm">Sultanate of Rûm</a> before the <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After Manzikert, a partial recovery (referred to as the Komnenian restoration) was made possible by the Komnenian dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M124_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-M124-123">[122]</a></sup> The first Komnenian emperor was <a href="/wiki/Isaac_I_Komnenos" title="Isaac I Komnenos">Isaac I</a> (1057–1059), after which the <a href="/wiki/Doukas" title="Doukas">Doukas dynasty</a> held power (1059–81). The Komnenoi attained power again under Alexios I in 1081. From the outset of his reign, Alexios faced a formidable attack by the Normans under Robert Guiscard and his son <a href="/wiki/Bohemond_I_of_Antioch" title="Bohemond I of Antioch">Bohemund of Taranto</a>, who captured <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dyrrhachium_(1081)" title="Battle of Dyrrhachium (1081)">Dyrrhachium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corfu" title="Corfu">Corfu</a>, and laid siege to <a href="/wiki/Larissa" title="Larissa">Larissa</a> in <a href="/wiki/Thessaly" title="Thessaly">Thessaly</a>. Robert Guiscard's death in 1085 temporarily eased the Norman problem. The following year, the Seljuq sultan died, and the sultanate was split by internal rivalries. By his own efforts, Alexios defeated the <a href="/wiki/Pechenegs" title="Pechenegs">Pechenegs</a>; they were caught by surprise and annihilated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Levounion" title="Battle of Levounion">Battle of Levounion</a> on 28 April 1091.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Br-124">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>Having achieved stability in the West, Alexios could turn his attention to the severe economic difficulties and the disintegration of the Empire's traditional defences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birkenmeier_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Birkenmeier-125">[124]</a></sup> However, he still did not have enough manpower to recover the lost territories in Asia Minor and to advance against the Seljuks. At the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Piacenza" title="Council of Piacenza">Council of Piacenza</a> in 1095, envoys from Alexios spoke to <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_II" title="Pope Urban II">Pope Urban II</a> about the suffering of the Christians of the East, and underscored that without help from the West they would continue to suffer under Muslim rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-126">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>Urban saw Alexios's request as a dual opportunity to cement Western Europe and reunite the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> under his rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-126">[125]</a></sup> On 27 November 1095, Pope Urban II called together the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Clermont" title="Council of Clermont">Council of Clermont</a>, and urged all those present to take up arms under the sign of the <a href="/wiki/Christian_cross" title="Christian cross">Cross</a> and launch an armed <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">pilgrimage</a> to recover Jerusalem and the East from the Muslims. The response in Western Europe was overwhelming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-Br-124">[123]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Histamenon_nomisma-Alexius_I-sb1776.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="219" data-file-width="500" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Histamenon_nomisma-Alexius_I-sb1776.jpg/220px-Histamenon_nomisma-Alexius_I-sb1776.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Histamenon_nomisma-Alexius_I-sb1776.jpg/330px-Histamenon_nomisma-Alexius_I-sb1776.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Histamenon_nomisma-Alexius_I-sb1776.jpg/440px-Histamenon_nomisma-Alexius_I-sb1776.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Histamenon_nomisma-Alexius_I-sb1776.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The brief first coinage of the <a href="/wiki/Thessaloniki" title="Thessaloniki">Thessaloniki</a> mint, opened by Alexios in September 1081, on his way to confront the invading Normans under Robert Guiscard</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alexios had anticipated help in the form of mercenary forces from the West, but he was totally unprepared for the immense and undisciplined force that soon arrived in Byzantine territory. It was no comfort to Alexios to learn that four of the eight leaders of the main body of the Crusade were Normans, among them Bohemund. Since the crusade had to pass through Constantinople, however, the Emperor had some control over it. He required its leaders to swear to restore to the empire any towns or territories they might reconquer from the Turks on their way to the Holy Land. In return, he gave them guides and a military escort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A261_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-A261-127">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>Alexios was able to recover a number of important cities and islands, and in fact much of western Asia Minor. Nevertheless, the Catholic/Latin crusaders believed their oaths were invalidated when Alexios did not help them during the siege of Antioch (he had in fact set out on the road to Antioch but had been persuaded to turn back by <a href="/wiki/Stephen,_Count_of_Blois" title="Stephen, Count of Blois">Stephen of Blois</a>, who assured him that all was lost and that the expedition had already failed).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A291_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-A291-128">[127]</a></sup> Bohemund, who had set himself up as <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Antioch" title="Prince of Antioch">Prince of Antioch</a>, briefly went to war with the Byzantines, but he agreed to become Alexios' vassal under the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Devol" title="Treaty of Devol">Treaty of Devol</a> in 1108, which marked the end of the Norman threat during Alexios' reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A348-358_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-A348-358-129">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="John_II.2C_Manuel_I_and_the_Second_Crusade">John II, Manuel I and the Second Crusade</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/John_II_Komnenos" title="John II Komnenos">John II Komnenos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manuel_I_Komnenos" title="Manuel I Komnenos">Manuel I Komnenos</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1099jerusalem.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="373" data-file-width="390" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/1099jerusalem.jpg/200px-1099jerusalem.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/1099jerusalem.jpg/300px-1099jerusalem.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5c/1099jerusalem.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1099jerusalem.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Medieval manuscript depicting the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jerusalem_(1099)" title="Siege of Jerusalem (1099)">Capture of Jerusalem</a> during the First Crusade</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alexios's son <a href="/wiki/John_II_Komnenos" title="John II Komnenos">John II Komnenos</a> succeeded him in 1118 and ruled until 1143. John was a pious and dedicated Emperor who was determined to undo the damage to the empire suffered at the Battle of Manzikert, half a century earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup> Famed for his piety and his remarkably mild and just reign, John was an exceptional example of a moral ruler at a time when cruelty was the norm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup> For this reason, he has been called the Byzantine <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a>.</p>
<p>During his twenty-five year reign, John made alliances with the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> in the West and decisively defeated the <a href="/wiki/Pechenegs" title="Pechenegs">Pechenegs</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Beroia" title="Battle of Beroia">Battle of Beroia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B90_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-B90-132">[131]</a></sup> He thwarted Hungarian and Serbian threats during the 1120s, and in 1130 he allied himself with the <a href="/wiki/List_of_German_monarchs" title="List of German monarchs">German emperor</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lothair_III" title="Lothair III">Lothair III</a> against the Norman king <a href="/wiki/Roger_II_of_Sicily" title="Roger II of Sicily">Roger II of Sicily</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECinnamus197674.E2.80.9375_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECinnamus197674.E2.80.9375-133">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the later part of his reign, John focused his activities on the East, personally leading numerous campaigns against the <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turks</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>. His campaigns fundamentally altered the balance of power in the East, forcing the Turks onto the defensive, while restoring many towns, fortresses, and cities across the peninsula to the Byzantines. He defeated the <a href="/wiki/Danishmends" title="Danishmends">Danishmend Emirate</a> of <a href="/wiki/Malatya" title="Malatya">Melitene</a> and reconquered all of <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a>, while forcing <a href="/wiki/Raymond_of_Poitiers" title="Raymond of Poitiers">Raymond of Poitiers</a>, Prince of Antioch, to recognise Byzantine suzerainty. In an effort to demonstrate the Emperor's role as the leader of the Christian world, John marched into the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a> at the head of the combined forces of the Empire and the <a href="/wiki/Crusader_states" title="Crusader states">Crusader states</a>; yet despite his great vigour pressing the campaign, his hopes were disappointed by the treachery of his Crusader allies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup> In 1142, John returned to press his claims to Antioch, but he died in the spring of 1143 following a hunting accident. Raymond was emboldened to invade Cilicia, but he was defeated and forced to go to Constantinople to beg mercy from the new Emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B326_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-B326-135">[134]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Byzantine_Empire,_c.1180.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="425" data-file-width="926" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/The_Byzantine_Empire%2C_c.1180.svg/300px-The_Byzantine_Empire%2C_c.1180.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/The_Byzantine_Empire%2C_c.1180.svg/450px-The_Byzantine_Empire%2C_c.1180.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/The_Byzantine_Empire%2C_c.1180.svg/600px-The_Byzantine_Empire%2C_c.1180.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Byzantine_Empire,_c.1180.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Byzantine Empire in orange, c. 1180, at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Komnenian_restoration" title="Komnenian restoration">Komnenian period</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>John's chosen heir was his fourth son, <a href="/wiki/Manuel_I_Komnenos" title="Manuel I Komnenos">Manuel I Komnenos</a>, who campaigned aggressively against his neighbours both in the west and in the east. In Palestine, Manuel allied with the Crusader <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Jerusalem" title="Kingdom of Jerusalem">Kingdom of Jerusalem</a> and sent a large fleet to participate in a combined invasion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimid_Egypt" title="Fatimid Egypt">Fatimid Egypt</a>. Manuel reinforced his position as overlord of the Crusader states, with his hegemony over Antioch and Jerusalem secured by agreement with <a href="/wiki/Raynald_of_Ch%C3%A2tillon" title="Raynald of Châtillon">Raynald</a>, Prince of Antioch, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amalric_I_of_Jerusalem" title="Amalric I of Jerusalem">Amalric</a>, King of Jerusalem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-S-136">[135]</a></sup> In an effort to restore Byzantine control over the ports of southern Italy, he sent an expedition to Italy in 1155, but disputes within the coalition led to the eventual failure of the campaign. Despite this military setback, Manuel's armies successfully invaded the Southern parts of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hungary" title="Kingdom of Hungary">Kingdom of Hungary</a> in 1167, defeating the Hungarians at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sirmium" title="Battle of Sirmium">Battle of Sirmium</a>. By 1168, nearly the whole of the eastern Adriatic coast lay in Manuel's hands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S372_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-S372-137">[136]</a></sup> Manuel made several alliances with the Pope and Western Christian kingdoms, and he successfully handled the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Crusade" title="Second Crusade">Second Crusade</a> through his empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M67_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-M67-138">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the east, however, Manuel suffered a major defeat in 1176 at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Myriokephalon" title="Battle of Myriokephalon">Battle of Myriokephalon</a>, against the Turks. Yet the losses were quickly recovered, and in the following year Manuel's forces inflicted a defeat upon a force of "picked Turks".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B129_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-B129-139">[138]</a></sup> The Byzantine commander John Vatatzes, who destroyed the Turkish invaders at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hyelion_and_Leimocheir" title="Battle of Hyelion and Leimocheir">Battle of Hyelion and Leimocheir</a>, not only brought troops from the capital but also was able to gather an army along the way, a sign that the Byzantine army remained strong and that the defensive program of western Asia Minor was still successful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B196_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-B196-140">[139]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="12th-century_Renaissance">12th-century Renaissance</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_civilisation_in_the_twelfth_century" title="Byzantine civilisation in the twelfth century">Byzantine civilisation in the twelfth century</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Komnenian_Byzantine_army" title="Komnenian Byzantine army">Komnenian Byzantine army</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meister_von_Nerezi_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1494" data-file-width="2024" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Meister_von_Nerezi_001.jpg/250px-Meister_von_Nerezi_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Meister_von_Nerezi_001.jpg/375px-Meister_von_Nerezi_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Meister_von_Nerezi_001.jpg/500px-Meister_von_Nerezi_001.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meister_von_Nerezi_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
'The Lamentation of Christ' (1164), a fresco from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Church_of_St._Panteleimon_(Nerezi)" title="Church of St. Panteleimon (Nerezi)">church of Saint Panteleimon</a> in Nerezi near Skopje; it is considered a superb example of 12th-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comnenus" title="Comnenus">Komnenian</a> art</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>John and Manuel pursued active military policies, and both deployed considerable resources on sieges and on city defences; aggressive fortification policies were at the heart of their imperial military policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B185-186_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-B185-186-141">[140]</a></sup> Despite the defeat at Myriokephalon, the policies of Alexios, John and Manuel resulted in vast territorial gains, increased frontier stability in Asia Minor, and secured the stabilisation of the Empire's European frontiers. From c. 1081 to c. 1180, the Komnenian army assured the Empire's security, enabling Byzantine civilisation to flourish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br1_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Br1-142">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>This allowed the Western provinces to achieve an economic revival that continued until the close of the century. It has been argued that Byzantium under the Komnenian rule was more prosperous than at any time since the Persian invasions of the 7th century. During the 12th century, population levels rose and extensive tracts of new agricultural land were brought into production. Archaeological evidence from both Europe and Asia Minor shows a considerable increase in the size of urban settlements, together with a notable upsurge in new towns. Trade was also flourishing; the Venetians, the <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoese</a> and others opened up the ports of the Aegean to commerce, shipping goods from the Crusader kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Outremer" title="Outremer">Outremer</a> and Fatimid Egypt to the west and trading with the Empire via Constantinople.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Day-143">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>In artistic terms, there was a revival in <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaic</a>, and regional schools of architecture began producing many distinctive styles that drew on a range of cultural influences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diehl_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Diehl-144">[143]</a></sup> During the 12th century, the Byzantines provided their model of early <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">humanism</a> as a renaissance of interest in classical authors. In <a href="/wiki/Eustathius_of_Thessalonica" title="Eustathius of Thessalonica">Eustathius of Thessalonica</a>, Byzantine humanism found its most characteristic expression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TM_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-TM-145">[144]</a></sup> In philosophy, there was resurgence of classical learning not seen since the 7th century, characterised by a significant increase in the publication of commentaries on classical works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-190-218_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-190-218-121">[120]</a></sup> In addition, the first transmission of classical Greek knowledge to the West occurred during the Komnenian period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-218_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-218-122">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_and_disintegration">Decline and disintegration</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Byzantine_Empire" title="Decline of the Byzantine Empire">Decline of the Byzantine Empire</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Angelid_dynasty">Angelid dynasty</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Angelos_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Angelos dynasty">Byzantine Empire under the Angelos dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantium-Angeloi.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="550" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b3/Byzantium-Angeloi.JPG/250px-Byzantium-Angeloi.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b3/Byzantium-Angeloi.JPG/375px-Byzantium-Angeloi.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b3/Byzantium-Angeloi.JPG/500px-Byzantium-Angeloi.JPG 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Byzantium-Angeloi.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Byzantium in the late Angeloi period</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Manuel's death on 24 September 1180 left his 11-year-old son <a href="/wiki/Alexios_II_Komnenos" title="Alexios II Komnenos">Alexios II Komnenos</a> on the throne. Alexios was highly incompetent at the office, but it was his mother, <a href="/wiki/Maria_of_Antioch" title="Maria of Antioch">Maria of Antioch</a>, and her Frankish background that made his regency unpopular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich291_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norwich291-146">[145]</a></sup> Eventually, <a href="/wiki/Andronikos_I_Komnenos" title="Andronikos I Komnenos">Andronikos I Komnenos</a>, a grandson of Alexios I, launched a revolt against his younger relative and managed to overthrow him in a violent <i>coup d'état</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich292_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norwich292-147">[146]</a></sup> Utilizing his good looks and his immense popularity with the army, he marched on to Constantinople in August 1182 <a href="/wiki/Massacre_of_the_Latins" title="Massacre of the Latins">and incited a massacre of the Latins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich292_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-Norwich292-147">[146]</a></sup> After eliminating his potential rivals, he had himself crowned as co-emperor in September 1183. He eliminated Alexios II, and took his 12-year-old wife <a href="/wiki/Agnes_of_France,_Byzantine_Empress" title="Agnes of France, Byzantine Empress">Agnes of France</a> for himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich292_147-2"><a href="#cite_note-Norwich292-147">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>Andronikos began his reign well; in particular, the measures he took to reform the government of the Empire have been praised by historians. According to <a href="/wiki/George_Ostrogorsky" title="George Ostrogorsky">George Ostrogorsky</a>, Andronikos was determined to root out corruption: Under his rule, the sale of offices ceased; selection was based on merit, rather than favouritism; officials were paid an adequate salary so as to reduce the temptation of bribery. In the provinces, Andronikos's reforms produced a speedy and marked improvement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M194_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-M194-148">[147]</a></sup> The aristocrats were infuriated against him, and to make matters worse, Andronikos seems to have become increasingly unbalanced; executions and violence became increasingly common, and his reign turned into a reign of terror.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[148]</a></sup> Andronikos seemed almost to seek the extermination of the aristocracy as a whole. The struggle against the aristocracy turned into wholesale slaughter, while the Emperor resorted to ever more ruthless measures to shore up his regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M194_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-M194-148">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite his military background, Andronikos failed to deal with <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Komnenos_of_Cyprus" title="Isaac Komnenos of Cyprus">Isaac Komnenos</a>, <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9la_III_of_Hungary" title="Béla III of Hungary">Béla III of Hungary</a> (r. 1172–1196) who reincorporated Croatian territories into Hungary, and <a href="/wiki/Stefan_Nemanja" title="Stefan Nemanja">Stephen Nemanja of Serbia</a> (r. 1166–1196) who declared his independence from the Byzantine Empire. Yet, none of these troubles would compare to <a href="/wiki/William_II_of_Sicily" title="William II of Sicily">William II of Sicily</a>'s (r. 1166–1189) invasion force of 300 ships and 80,000 men, arriving in 1185.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich293_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norwich293-150">[149]</a></sup> Andronikos mobilised a small fleet of 100 ships to defend the capital, but other than that he was indifferent to the populace. He was finally overthrown when <a href="/wiki/Isaac_II_Angelos" title="Isaac II Angelos">Isaac Angelos</a>, surviving an imperial assassination attempt, seized power with the aid of the people and had Andronikos killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich294-295_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norwich294-295-151">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>The reign of Isaac II, and more so that of his brother <a href="/wiki/Alexios_III_Angelos" title="Alexios III Angelos">Alexios III</a>, saw the collapse of what remained of the centralised machinery of Byzantine government and defence. Although the Normans were driven out of Greece, in 1186 the <a href="/wiki/Vlachs" title="Vlachs">Vlachs</a> and Bulgars began a rebellion that led to the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Bulgarian_Empire" title="Second Bulgarian Empire">Second Bulgarian Empire</a>. The internal policy of the Angeloi was characterised by the squandering of the public treasure and fiscal maladministration. Imperial authority was severely weakened, and the growing power vacuum at the center of the Empire encouraged fragmentation. There is evidence that some Komnenian heirs had set up a semi-independent state in <a href="/wiki/Trabzon" title="Trabzon">Trebizond</a> before 1204.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-152">[151]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Vasiliev_(historian)" title="Alexander Vasiliev (historian)">Alexander Vasiliev</a>, "the dynasty of the Angeloi, Greek in its origin, ... accelerated the ruin of the Empire, already weakened without and disunited within."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VA_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-VA-153">[152]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fourth_Crusade">Fourth Crusade</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Fourth Crusade</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1820" data-file-width="2223" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg/200px-Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg/300px-Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg/400px-Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The Entry of the Crusaders into Constantinople</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix" title="Eugène Delacroix">Eugène Delacroix</a> (1840).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1198, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_III" title="Pope Innocent III">Pope Innocent III</a> broached the subject of a new crusade through <a href="/wiki/Papal_legate" title="Papal legate">legates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Encyclical" title="Encyclical">encyclical letters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich299_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norwich299-154">[153]</a></sup> The stated intent of the crusade was to conquer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Arab_Egypt#Ayyubid_period" title="History of Arab Egypt">Egypt</a>, now the centre of Muslim power in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>. The crusader army that arrived at <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Venice</a> in the summer of 1202 was somewhat smaller than had been anticipated, and there were not sufficient funds to pay the Venetians, whose fleet was hired by the crusaders to take them to Egypt. Venetian policy under the ageing and blind but still ambitious <a href="/wiki/Doge_of_Venice" title="Doge of Venice">Doge</a> <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Dandolo" title="Enrico Dandolo">Enrico Dandolo</a> was potentially at variance with that of the Pope and the crusaders, because Venice was closely related commercially with Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br4Cr_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-Br4Cr-155">[154]</a></sup> The crusaders accepted the suggestion that in lieu of payment they assist the Venetians in the capture of the (Christian) port of <a href="/wiki/Zadar#History" title="Zadar">Zara</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia" title="Dalmatia">Dalmatia</a> (vassal city of Venice, which had rebelled and placed itself under Hungary's protection in 1186).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrC_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrC-156">[155]</a></sup> The city fell in November 1202 after a brief <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Zara" title="Siege of Zara">siege</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[156]</a></sup> Innocent tried to forbid this political attack on a Christian city, but was ignored. Reluctant to jeopardise his own agenda for the Crusade, he gave conditional absolution to the crusaders—not, however, to the Venetians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br4Cr_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-Br4Cr-155">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the death of <a href="/wiki/Theobald_III,_Count_of_Champagne" title="Theobald III, Count of Champagne">Theobald III, Count of Champagne</a>, the leadership of the Crusade passed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boniface_of_Montferrat" title="Boniface of Montferrat">Boniface of Montferrat</a>, a friend of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/House_of_Hohenstaufen" title="House of Hohenstaufen">Hohenstaufen</a> <a href="/wiki/Philip_of_Swabia" title="Philip of Swabia">Philip of Swabia</a>. Both Boniface and Philip had married into the Byzantine Imperial family. In fact, Philip's brother-in-law, <a href="/wiki/Alexios_IV_Angelos" title="Alexios IV Angelos">Alexios Angelos</a>, son of the deposed and blinded Emperor <a href="/wiki/Isaac_II_Angelos" title="Isaac II Angelos">Isaac II Angelos</a>, had appeared in Europe seeking aid and had made contacts with the crusaders. Alexios offered to reunite the Byzantine church with Rome, pay the crusaders 200,000 silver marks, join the crusade and provide all the supplies they needed to get to Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich301_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norwich301-158">[157]</a></sup> Innocent was aware of a plan to divert the Crusade to Constantinople and forbade any attack on the city, but the papal letter arrived after the fleets had left Zara.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Crusader_sack_of_Constantinople_.281204.29">Crusader sack of Constantinople (1204)</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(1203)" title="Siege of Constantinople (1203)">Siege of Constantinople (1203)</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(1204)" title="Siege of Constantinople (1204)">Siege of Constantinople (1204)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantium1215.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="550" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Byzantium1215.jpg/250px-Byzantium1215.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Byzantium1215.jpg/375px-Byzantium1215.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Byzantium1215.jpg/500px-Byzantium1215.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Byzantium1215.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The partition of the empire following the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Fourth Crusade</a>, c. 1204.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The crusaders arrived at Constantinople in the summer of 1203 and quickly attacked, started a major fire that damaged large parts of the city, and briefly seized control. Alexios III fled from the capital, and Alexios Angelos was elevated to the throne as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexios_IV" title="Alexios IV">Alexios IV</a> along with his blind father Isaac. However, Alexios IV and Isaac II were unable to keep their promises and were deposed by Alexios V. The crusaders again took the city on 13 April 1204, and Constantinople was subjected to pillage and massacre by the rank and file for three days. Many priceless icons, relics, and other objects later turned up in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>, a large number in Venice. According to Choniates, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prostitute" title="Prostitute">prostitute</a> was even set up on the Patriarchal throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NC_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-NC-159">[158]</a></sup> When Innocent III heard of the conduct of his crusaders, he castigated them in no uncertain terms. But the situation was beyond his control, especially after his legate, on his own initiative, had absolved the crusaders from their vow to proceed to the Holy Land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br4Cr_155-2"><a href="#cite_note-Br4Cr-155">[154]</a></sup> When order had been restored, the crusaders and the Venetians proceeded to implement their agreement; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baldwin_I_of_Constantinople" title="Baldwin I of Constantinople">Baldwin of Flanders</a> was elected Emperor of a new <a href="/wiki/Latin_Empire" title="Latin Empire">Latin Empire</a>, and the Venetian <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Morosini" title="Thomas Morosini">Thomas Morosini</a> was chosen as Patriarch. The lands divided up among the leaders included most of the former Byzantine possessions, though resistance would continue through the Byzantine remnants of the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Nicaea" title="Empire of Nicaea">Nicaea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Trebizond" title="Empire of Trebizond">Trebizond</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Despotate_of_Epirus" title="Despotate of Epirus">Epirus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br4Cr_155-3"><a href="#cite_note-Br4Cr-155">[154]</a></sup> Although Venice was more interested in commerce than conquering territory, it took key areas of Constantinople, and the Doge took the title of "<i>Lord of a Quarter and Half a Quarter of the Roman Empire</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fall">Fall</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Empire_in_exile">Empire in exile</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latinokratia" title="Latinokratia">Latinokratia</a>.</div>
<p>After the sack of Constantinople in 1204 by Latin crusaders, two Byzantine successor states were established: the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Nicaea" title="Empire of Nicaea">Empire of Nicaea</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Despotate_of_Epirus" title="Despotate of Epirus">Despotate of Epirus</a>. A third, the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Trebizond" title="Empire of Trebizond">Empire of Trebizond</a>, was created by <a href="/wiki/Alexios_I_of_Trebizond" title="Alexios I of Trebizond">Alexios I of Trebizond</a> a few weeks before the sack of Constantinople. Of the three successor states, Epirus and Nicaea stood the best chance of reclaiming Constantinople. The Nicaean Empire struggled to survive the next few decades, however, and by the mid-13th century it had lost much of southern Anatolia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup> The weakening of the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Rum" title="Sultanate of Rum">Sultanate of Rûm</a> following the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_K%C3%B6se_Da%C4%9F" title="Battle of Köse Dağ">Mongol invasion in 1242–43</a> allowed many <a href="/wiki/Anatolian_beyliks" title="Anatolian beyliks">beyliks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ghazi_(warrior)" title="Ghazi (warrior)">ghazis</a> to set up their own principalities in Anatolia, weakening the Byzantine hold on Asia Minor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup> In time, one of the Beys, <a href="/wiki/Osman_I" title="Osman I">Osman I</a>, created an empire that would eventually conquer Constantinople. However, the Mongol invasion also gave Nicaea a temporary respite from Seljuk attacks, allowing it to concentrate on the Latin Empire to its north.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reconquest_of_Constantinople">Reconquest of Constantinople</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Palaiologos_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Palaiologos dynasty">Byzantine Empire under the Palaiologos dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1263_Mediterranean_Sea.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="424" data-file-width="925" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/1263_Mediterranean_Sea.svg/300px-1263_Mediterranean_Sea.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/1263_Mediterranean_Sea.svg/450px-1263_Mediterranean_Sea.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/1263_Mediterranean_Sea.svg/600px-1263_Mediterranean_Sea.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1263_Mediterranean_Sea.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Byzantine Empire c. 1263.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Empire of Nicaea, founded by the <a href="/wiki/Laskaris" title="Laskaris">Laskarid dynasty</a>, managed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recapture_of_Constantinople" title="Recapture of Constantinople">reclaim Constantinople</a> from the Latins in 1261 and defeat Epirus. This led to a short-lived revival of Byzantine fortunes under <a href="/wiki/Michael_VIII_Palaiologos" title="Michael VIII Palaiologos">Michael VIII Palaiologos</a>, but the war-ravaged Empire was ill-equipped to deal with the enemies that now surrounded it. To maintain his campaigns against the Latins, Michael pulled troops from Asia Minor and levied crippling taxes on the peasantry, causing much resentment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup> Massive construction projects were completed in Constantinople to repair the damage of the Fourth Crusade, but none of these initiatives was of any comfort to the farmers in Asia Minor suffering raids from Muslim ghazis.</p>
<p>Rather than holding on to his possessions in Asia Minor, Michael chose to expand the Empire, gaining only short-term success. To avoid another sacking of the capital by the Latins, he forced the Church to submit to Rome, again a temporary solution for which the peasantry hated Michael and Constantinople.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[163]</a></sup> The efforts of <a href="/wiki/Andronikos_II_Palaiologos" title="Andronikos II Palaiologos">Andronikos II</a> and later his grandson <a href="/wiki/Andronikos_III_Palaiologos" title="Andronikos III Palaiologos">Andronikos III</a> marked Byzantium's last genuine attempts in restoring the glory of the Empire. However, the use of mercenaries by Andronikos II would often backfire, with the <a href="/wiki/Catalan_Company" title="Catalan Company">Catalan Company</a> ravaging the countryside and increasing resentment towards Constantinople.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[164]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_of_the_Ottomans_and_fall_of_Constantinople">Rise of the Ottomans and fall of Constantinople</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Ottoman_Wars" title="Byzantine–Ottoman Wars">Byzantine–Ottoman Wars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">Fall of Constantinople</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Constantinople_1453.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="365" height="321" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Constantinople_1453.jpg/220px-Constantinople_1453.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Constantinople_1453.jpg/330px-Constantinople_1453.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fa/Constantinople_1453.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Constantinople_1453.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">siege of Constantinople</a> in 1453, depicted in a 15th-century French miniature.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The situation became worse for Byzantium during the civil wars after Andronikos III died. A <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_civil_war_of_1341%E2%80%931347" title="Byzantine civil war of 1341–1347">six-year-long civil war</a> devastated the empire, allowing the Serbian ruler <a href="/wiki/Stefan_Du%C5%A1an" title="Stefan Dušan">Stefan Dušan</a> (r. 1331–1346) to overrun most of the Empire's remaining territory and establish a <a href="/wiki/Serbian_Empire" title="Serbian Empire">Serbian Empire</a>. In 1354, an earthquake at <a href="/wiki/Gelibolu" title="Gelibolu">Gallipoli</a> devastated the fort, allowing the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottomans</a> (who were hired as mercenaries during the civil war by <a href="/wiki/John_VI_Kantakouzenos" title="John VI Kantakouzenos">John VI Kantakouzenos</a>) to establish themselves in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[165]</a></sup> By the time the Byzantine civil wars had ended, the Ottomans had defeated the Serbians and subjugated them as vassals. Following the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kosovo" title="Battle of Kosovo">Battle of Kosovo</a>, much of the Balkans became dominated by the Ottomans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Byzantine emperors appealed to the West for help, but the Pope would only consider sending aid in return for a reunion of the Eastern Orthodox Church with the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">See of Rome</a>. Church unity was considered, and occasionally accomplished by imperial decree, but the Orthodox citizenry and clergy intensely resented the authority of Rome and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_Rite" title="Latin Rite">Latin Rite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[167]</a></sup> Some Western troops arrived to bolster the Christian defence of Constantinople, but most Western rulers, distracted by their own affairs, did nothing as the Ottomans picked apart the remaining Byzantine territories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R84-85_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-R84-85-169">[168]</a></sup></p>
<p>Constantinople by this stage was underpopulated and dilapidated. The population of the city had collapsed so severely that it was now little more than a cluster of villages separated by fields. On 2 April 1453, <a href="/wiki/Mehmed_the_Conqueror" title="Mehmed the Conqueror">Sultan Mehmed</a>'s army of 80,000 men and large numbers of irregulars laid siege to the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R84-86_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-R84-86-170">[169]</a></sup> Despite a desperate last-ditch defence of the city by the massively outnumbered Christian forces (c. 7,000 men, 2,000 of whom were foreign),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R84-85_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-R84-85-169">[168]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">Constantinople finally fell</a> to the Ottomans after a two-month siege on 29 May 1453. The last Byzantine emperor, <a href="/wiki/Constantine_XI_Palaiologos" title="Constantine XI Palaiologos">Constantine XI Palaiologos</a>, was last seen casting off his imperial regalia and throwing himself into hand-to-hand combat after the walls of the city were taken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[170]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_aftermath">Political aftermath</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eastern_Mediterranean_1450.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2395" data-file-width="2529" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Eastern_Mediterranean_1450.svg/200px-Eastern_Mediterranean_1450.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Eastern_Mediterranean_1450.svg/300px-Eastern_Mediterranean_1450.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Eastern_Mediterranean_1450.svg/400px-Eastern_Mediterranean_1450.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eastern_Mediterranean_1450.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Eastern Mediterranean just before the fall of Constantinople</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By the time of the fall of Constantinople, the only remaining territory of the Byzantine Empire was the <a href="/wiki/Despotate_of_the_Morea" title="Despotate of the Morea">Despotate of the Morea</a> (<a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnese</a>), which was ruled by brothers of the last Emperor, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Palaiologos" title="Thomas Palaiologos">Thomas Palaiologos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Demetrios_Palaiologos" title="Demetrios Palaiologos">Demetrios Palaiologos</a>. The Despotate continued on as an independent state by paying an annual tribute to the Ottomans. Incompetent rule, failure to pay the annual tribute and a revolt against the Ottomans finally led to Mehmed II's invasion of Morea in May 1460. Demetrios asked the Ottomans to invade and drive Thomas out. Thomas fled. The Ottomans moved through the Morea and conquered virtually the entire Despotate by the summer. Demetrios thought the Morea would be restored to him to rule, but it was incorporated into the Ottoman fold.</p>
<p>A few holdouts remained for a time. The island of <a href="/wiki/Monemvasia" title="Monemvasia">Monemvasia</a> refused to surrender and it was first ruled for a short time by an Aragonese corsair. When the population drove him out they obtained the consent of Thomas to place themselves under the Pope's protection before the end of 1460. The <a href="/wiki/Mani_Peninsula" title="Mani Peninsula">Mani Peninsula</a>, on the Morea's south end, resisted under a loose coalition of the local clans and then that area came under Venice's rule. The very last holdout was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salmeniko" title="Salmeniko">Salmeniko</a>, in the Morea's northwest. <a href="/wiki/Graitzas_Palaiologos" title="Graitzas Palaiologos">Graitzas Palaiologos</a> was the military commander there, stationed at <a href="/wiki/Salmeniko_Castle" title="Salmeniko Castle">Salmeniko Castle</a>. While the town eventually surrendered, Graitzas and his garrison and some town residents held out in the castle until July 1461, when they escaped and reached Venetian territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[171]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_imperial_flag,_14th_century.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="503" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Byzantine_imperial_flag%2C_14th_century.svg/200px-Byzantine_imperial_flag%2C_14th_century.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Byzantine_imperial_flag%2C_14th_century.svg/300px-Byzantine_imperial_flag%2C_14th_century.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Byzantine_imperial_flag%2C_14th_century.svg/400px-Byzantine_imperial_flag%2C_14th_century.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_imperial_flag,_14th_century.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Byzantine_flags_and_insignia" title="Byzantine flags and insignia">Flag of the late Empire</a> under the Palaiologoi, sporting the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetragrammic_cross" title="Tetragrammic cross">tetragrammic cross</a> symbol of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palaiologos_dynasty" title="Palaiologos dynasty">Palaiologos dynasty</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Trebizond" title="Empire of Trebizond">Empire of Trebizond</a>, which had split away from the Byzantine Empire just weeks before Constantinople was taken by the Crusaders in 1204, became the last remnant and last de facto successor state to the Byzantine Empire. Efforts by the <a href="/wiki/David_of_Trebizond" title="David of Trebizond">Emperor David</a> to recruit European powers for an anti-Ottoman crusade provoked war between the Ottomans and Trebizond in the summer of 1461. After a month-long siege, David surrendered the city of Trebizond on 14 August 1461. The Empire of Trebizond's Crimean principality, the <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Theodoro" title="Principality of Theodoro">Principality of Theodoro</a> (part of the <a href="/wiki/Perateia" title="Perateia">Perateia</a>), lasted another 14 years, falling to the Ottomans in December 1475.</p>
<p>A nephew of the last Emperor, Constantine XI, <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Palaiologos" title="Andreas Palaiologos">Andreas Palaiologos</a> claimed to have inherited the title of <a href="/wiki/List_of_Byzantine_emperors" title="List of Byzantine emperors">Byzantine Emperor</a>. He lived in the Morea until its fall in 1460, then escaped to Rome where he lived under the protection of the <a href="/wiki/Papal_States" title="Papal States">Papal States</a> for the remainder of his life. Since the office of emperor had never been technically hereditary, Andreas' claim would have been without merit under Byzantine law. However, the Empire had vanished, and Western states generally followed the Roman-church-sanctioned principles of hereditary sovereignty.
