Seeking a life in the west, Andreas styled himself <i>Imperator Constantinopolitanus</i> ("Emperor of Constantinople"), and sold his succession rights to both <a href="/wiki/Charles_VIII_of_France" title="Charles VIII of France">Charles VIII of France</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Monarchs" title="Catholic Monarchs">Catholic Monarchs</a>. However, no one ever invoked the title after Andreas's death.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_XI" title="Constantine XI">Constantine XI</a> died without producing an heir, and had Constantinople not fallen he might have been succeeded by the sons of his deceased elder brother, who were taken into the palace service of Mehmed II after the fall of Constantinople. The oldest boy, re-christened as Has Murad, became a personal favorite of Mehmed and served as Beylerbey (Governor-General) of the Balkans. The younger son, renamed <a href="/wiki/Mesih_Pasha" title="Mesih Pasha">Mesih Pasha</a>, became Admiral of the Ottoman fleet and Sancak Beg (Governor) of the Province of Gallipoli. He eventually served twice as Grand Vizier under Mehmed's son, <a href="/wiki/Bayezid_II" title="Bayezid II">Bayezid II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[172]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mehmed II and his successors continued to consider themselves heirs to the Roman Empire until <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Dissolution of the Ottoman Empire">the demise of the Ottoman Empire</a> in the early 20th century. They considered that they had simply shifted its religious basis as Constantine had done before, and they continued to refer to their conquered Eastern Roman inhabitants (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_Christians" title="Orthodox Christians">Orthodox Christians</a>) as <a href="/wiki/R%C3%BBm" title="Rûm">Rûm</a>. Meanwhile, the <a href="/wiki/Danubian_Principalities" title="Danubian Principalities">Danubian Principalities</a> (whose rulers also considered themselves the heirs of the Eastern Roman Emperors<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[173]</a></sup>) harboured Orthodox refugees, including some Byzantine nobles.</p>
<p>At his death, the role of the emperor as a patron of Eastern Orthodoxy was claimed by <a href="/wiki/Ivan_III_of_Russia" title="Ivan III of Russia">Ivan III</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grand_duke" title="Grand duke">Grand duke</a> of <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Moscow" title="Grand Duchy of Moscow">Muscovy</a>. He had married Andreas' sister, <a href="/wiki/Sophia_Palaiologina" title="Sophia Palaiologina">Sophia Paleologue</a>, whose grandson, <a href="/wiki/Ivan_the_Terrible" title="Ivan the Terrible">Ivan IV</a>, would become the first <a href="/wiki/Tsar" title="Tsar">Tsar</a> of Russia (<i>tsar</i>, or <i>czar</i>, meaning <i>caesar</i>, is a term traditionally applied by Slavs to the Byzantine Emperors). Their successors supported the idea that Moscow was the proper heir to Rome and Constantinople. The idea of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> as the successive <a href="/wiki/Third_Rome" title="Third Rome">Third Rome</a> was kept alive until its demise with the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution" title="Russian Revolution">Russian Revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[174]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_economy" title="Byzantine economy">Byzantine economy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_silk" title="Byzantine silk">Byzantine silk</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sino-Roman_relations" title="Sino-Roman relations">Sino-Roman relations</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_coin_of_Contantius_II_337_361_found_in_Karghalik.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1248" data-file-width="1231" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Bronze_coin_of_Contantius_II_337_361_found_in_Karghalik.jpg/220px-Bronze_coin_of_Contantius_II_337_361_found_in_Karghalik.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Bronze_coin_of_Contantius_II_337_361_found_in_Karghalik.jpg/330px-Bronze_coin_of_Contantius_II_337_361_found_in_Karghalik.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Bronze_coin_of_Contantius_II_337_361_found_in_Karghalik.jpg/440px-Bronze_coin_of_Contantius_II_337_361_found_in_Karghalik.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

<p>The Byzantine economy was among the most advanced in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean_region" title="Mediterranean region">Mediterranean</a> for many centuries. Europe, in particular, could not match Byzantine economic strength until late in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>. <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> operated as a prime hub in a trading network that at various times extended across nearly all of <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, in particular as the primary western terminus of the famous <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>. Until the first half of the 6th century and in sharp contrast with the decaying West, the Byzantine economy was flourishing and resilient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Justinian" title="Plague of Justinian">Plague of Justinian</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab_conquests" title="Arab conquests">Arab conquests</a> would represent a substantial reversal of fortunes contributing to a period of stagnation and <a href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Byzantine_Empire" title="Decline of the Byzantine Empire">decline</a>. Isaurian reforms and, in particular, <a href="/wiki/Constantine_V" title="Constantine V">Constantine V</a>'s repopulation, public works and tax measures, marked the beginning of a revival that continued until 1204, despite territorial contraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[176]</a></sup> From the 10th century until the end of the 12th, the Byzantine Empire projected an image of luxury and travellers were impressed by the wealth accumulated in the capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M532_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-M532-178">[177]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Fourth Crusade</a> resulted in the disruption of Byzantine manufacturing and the commercial dominance of the Western Europeans in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">eastern Mediterranean</a>, events that amounted to an economic catastrophe for the Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M532_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-M532-178">[177]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palaiologoi" title="Palaiologoi">Palaiologoi</a> tried to revive the economy, but the late Byzantine state would not gain full control of either the foreign or domestic economic forces. Gradually, it also lost its influence on the modalities of trade and the price mechanisms, and its control over the outflow of precious metals and, according to some scholars, even over the minting of coins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M806_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-M806-179">[178]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the economic foundations of Byzantium was trade, fostered by the maritime character of the Empire. Textiles must have been by far the most important item of export; <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silks</a> were certainly imported into Egypt, and appeared also in Bulgaria, and the West.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L723_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-L723-180">[179]</a></sup> The state strictly controlled both the internal and the international trade, and retained the monopoly of issuing <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_coinage" title="Byzantine coinage">coinage</a>, maintaining a durable and flexible monetary system adaptable to trade needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L3-4_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-L3-4-181">[180]</a></sup></p>
<p>The government attempted to exercise formal control over interest rates, and set the parameters for the activity of the <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guilds</a> and corporations, in which it had a special interest. The emperor and his officials intervened at times of crisis to ensure the provisioning of the capital, and to keep down the price of cereals. Finally, the government often collected part of the surplus through taxation, and put it back into circulation, through redistribution in the form of salaries to state officials, or in the form of investment in public works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L3-4_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-L3-4-181">[180]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Science.2C_medicine_and_law">Science, medicine and law</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_science" title="Byzantine science">Byzantine science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_medicine" title="Byzantine medicine">Byzantine medicine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_law" title="Byzantine law">Byzantine law</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_Interior_Panorama.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3047" data-file-width="3856" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Hagia_Sophia_Interior_Panorama.jpg/250px-Hagia_Sophia_Interior_Panorama.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Hagia_Sophia_Interior_Panorama.jpg/375px-Hagia_Sophia_Interior_Panorama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Hagia_Sophia_Interior_Panorama.jpg/500px-Hagia_Sophia_Interior_Panorama.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_Interior_Panorama.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Interior panorama of the <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, the patriarchal <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">basilica</a> in Constantinople designed 537 CE by <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Miletus" title="Isidore of Miletus">Isidore of Miletus</a>, the first compiler of Archimedes' various works. The influence of Archimedes' principles of solid geometry is evident.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ViennaDioscoridesFolio3v7Physicians.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="600" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/ViennaDioscoridesFolio3v7Physicians.jpg/120px-ViennaDioscoridesFolio3v7Physicians.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/ViennaDioscoridesFolio3v7Physicians.jpg/180px-ViennaDioscoridesFolio3v7Physicians.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/ViennaDioscoridesFolio3v7Physicians.jpg/240px-ViennaDioscoridesFolio3v7Physicians.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ViennaDioscoridesFolio3v7Physicians.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The frontispiece of the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Dioscurides" title="Vienna Dioscurides">Vienna Dioscurides</a>, which shows a set of seven famous physicians</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The writings of <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">Classical antiquity</a> were cultivated and extended in Byzantium. Therefore, Byzantine science was in every period closely connected with <a href="/wiki/Ancient_philosophy" title="Ancient philosophy">ancient philosophy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[181]</a></sup> In the field of engineering <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Miletus" title="Isidore of Miletus">Isidore of Miletus</a>, the Greek mathematician and architect of the <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, produced the first compilation of <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a>' works c. 530, and it is through this manuscript tradition, kept alive by the school of mathematics and engineering founded c. 850 during the "Byzantine Renaissance" by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leo_the_Geometer" title="Leo the Geometer">Leo the Geometer</a>, that such works are known today (see <a href="/wiki/Archimedes_Palimpsest" title="Archimedes Palimpsest">Archimedes Palimpsest</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[182]</a></sup> Indeed, geometry and its applications (architecture and engineering instruments of war) remained a specialty of the Byzantines. In medicine the works of Byzantine doctors, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vienna_Dioscorides" title="Vienna Dioscorides">Vienna Dioscorides</a> (6th century), and works of <a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Aegina" title="Paul of Aegina">Paul of Aegina</a> (7th century) and <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Myrepsos" title="Nicholas Myrepsos">Nicholas Myrepsos</a> (late 13th century), continued to be used as the authoritative texts by Europeans through the Renaissance, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Byzantine_inventions" title="List of Byzantine inventions">several technological advancements</a>, including the pendentive dome and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Fire" title="Greek Fire">Greek Fire</a>, are attributed to the Byzantines.</p>
<p>Although at various times the Byzantines made magnificent achievements in the application of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Science_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Science in the Middle Ages">sciences</a>, and are responsible for preserving much of ancient knowledge, some authors have argued that Byzantine scholars made few novel contributions to science in terms of developing new theories or extending the ideas of classical authors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[183]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the final century of the Empire, <a href="/wiki/Greek_scholars_in_the_Renaissance" title="Greek scholars in the Renaissance">refugee Byzantine scholars</a> were principally responsible for carrying, in person and in writing, ancient Greek grammatical, literary studies, mathematical, and astronomical knowledge to early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renaissance_Italy" title="Renaissance Italy">Renaissance Italy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[184]</a></sup> During this period, <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematical sciences</a> were taught in Trebizond; medicine attracted the interest of almost all scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TM189_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-TM189-186">[185]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the field of law, <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a>'s reforms had a clear effect on the evolution of <a href="/wiki/Jurisprudence" title="Jurisprudence">jurisprudence</a>, with his <a href="/wiki/Corpus_Juris_Civilis" title="Corpus Juris Civilis">Corpus Juris Civilis</a> becoming the basis for revived Roman law in the West, while Leo III's <i>Ecloga</i> influenced the formation of legal institutions in the Slavic world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[186]</a></sup> In the 10th century, <a href="/wiki/Leo_VI_the_Wise" title="Leo VI the Wise">Leo VI the Wise</a> achieved the complete codification of the whole of Byzantine law in Greek with the <a href="/wiki/Basilika" title="Basilika">Basilika</a>, which became the foundation of all subsequent Byzantine law with an influence extending through to modern Balkan legal codes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-97-98_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-97-98-103">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/State_church_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="State church of the Roman Empire">State church of the Roman Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aya_sofya.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="688" data-file-width="1024" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Aya_sofya.jpg/250px-Aya_sofya.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Aya_sofya.jpg/375px-Aya_sofya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Aya_sofya.jpg/500px-Aya_sofya.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aya_sofya.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
As a symbol and expression of the universal prestige of the Patriarchate of Constantinople, Justinian built the Church of the Holy Wisdom of God, <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, which was completed in the short period of four and a half years (532–537)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Santa_Sofia_-_Mosaic_de_Joan_II_Comn%C3%A8_i_la_seva_esposa,_Irene.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1408" data-file-width="2886" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Santa_Sofia_-_Mosaic_de_Joan_II_Comn%C3%A8_i_la_seva_esposa%2C_Irene.JPG/220px-Santa_Sofia_-_Mosaic_de_Joan_II_Comn%C3%A8_i_la_seva_esposa%2C_Irene.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Santa_Sofia_-_Mosaic_de_Joan_II_Comn%C3%A8_i_la_seva_esposa%2C_Irene.JPG/330px-Santa_Sofia_-_Mosaic_de_Joan_II_Comn%C3%A8_i_la_seva_esposa%2C_Irene.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Santa_Sofia_-_Mosaic_de_Joan_II_Comn%C3%A8_i_la_seva_esposa%2C_Irene.JPG/440px-Santa_Sofia_-_Mosaic_de_Joan_II_Comn%C3%A8_i_la_seva_esposa%2C_Irene.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Santa_Sofia_-_Mosaic_de_Joan_II_Comn%C3%A8_i_la_seva_esposa,_Irene.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaic</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a> of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> (modern Istanbul), depicting <a href="/wiki/Mary,_mother_of_Jesus" title="Mary, mother of Jesus">Mary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>, flanked by <a href="/wiki/John_II_Komnenos" title="John II Komnenos">John II Komnenos</a> (left) and his wife <a href="/wiki/Irene_of_Hungary" title="Irene of Hungary">Irene of Hungary</a> (right), 12th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Byzantine Empire was a <a href="/wiki/Theocracy" title="Theocracy">theocracy</a>, said to be ruled by <a href="/wiki/God_in_Christianity" title="God in Christianity">God</a> working through the Emperor. Jennifer Fretland VanVoorst argues, "The Byzantine Empire became a theocracy in the sense that Christian values and ideals were the foundation of the empire's political ideals and heavily entwined with its political goals."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[187]</a></sup> Steven Runciman says in his book on <i>The Byzantine Theocracy</i> (2004):</p>
<dl>
<dd>The constitution of the Byzantine Empire was based on the conviction that it was the earthly copy of the Kingdom of Heaven. Just as God ruled in Heaven, so the Emperor, made in his image, should rule on earth and carry out his commandments ... It saw itself as a universal empire. Ideally, it should embrace all the peoples of the Earth who, ideally, should all be members of the one true Christian Church, its own Orthodox Church. Just as man was made in God's image, so man's kingdom on Earth was made in the image of the Kingdom of Heaven."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[188]</a></sup> The survival of the Empire in the East assured an active role of the Emperor in the affairs of the Church. The Byzantine state inherited from pagan times the administrative, and financial routine of administering religious affairs, and this routine was applied to the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Church" title="Christian Church">Christian Church</a>. Following the pattern set by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eusebius_of_Caesarea" title="Eusebius of Caesarea">Eusebius of Caesarea</a>, the Byzantines viewed the Emperor as a representative or messenger of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Christ</a>, responsible particularly for the propagation of Christianity among pagans, and for the "externals" of the religion, such as administration and finances. As <a href="/wiki/Cyril_Mango" title="Cyril Mango">Cyril Mango</a> points out, the Byzantine political thinking can be summarised in the motto "One God, one empire, one religion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M108_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-M108-190">[189]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>The imperial role in the affairs of the Church never developed into a fixed, legally defined system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M14_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-M14-191">[190]</a></sup> With the decline of Rome, and internal dissension in the other Eastern Patriarchates, the Church of Constantinople became, between the 6th and 11th centuries, the richest and most influential center of <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M19_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-M19-192">[191]</a></sup> Even when the Empire was reduced to only a shadow of its former self, the Church continued to exercise significant influence both inside and outside of the imperial frontiers. As <a href="/wiki/George_Ostrogorsky" title="George Ostrogorsky">George Ostrogorsky</a> points out:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople">Patriarchate of Constantinople</a> remained the center of the Orthodox world, with subordinate <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_bishop" title="Metropolitan bishop">metropolitan sees</a> and archbishoprics in the territory of Asia Minor and the Balkans, now lost to Byzantium, as well as in <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, Russia and <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>. The Church remained the most stable element in the Byzantine Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M130_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-M130-193">[192]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The official state Christian doctrine was determined by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_seven_ecumenical_councils" title="First seven ecumenical councils">first seven ecumenical councils</a>, and it was then the emperor's duty to impose it to his subjects. An imperial decree of 388, which was later incorporated into the <i>Codex Justinianus</i>, orders the population of the Empire "to assume the name of Catholic Christians", and regards all those who will not abide by the law as "mad and foolish persons"; as followers of "heretical dogmas".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[193]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite imperial decrees and the stringent stance of the <a href="/wiki/State_church_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="State church of the Roman Empire">state church</a> itself, which came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a> or <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">Eastern Christianity</a>, the latter never represented all Christians in Byzantium. Mango believes that, in the early stages of the Empire, the "mad and foolish persons", those labelled "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heretics" title="Heretics">heretics</a>" by the state church, were the majority of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M109_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-M109-195">[194]</a></sup> Besides the <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">pagans</a>, who existed until the end of the 6th century, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jews_of_the_Byzantine_Empire" title="Jews of the Byzantine Empire">Jews</a>, there were many followers – sometimes even emperors – of various Christian doctrines, such as <a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">Nestorianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monophysitism" title="Monophysitism">Monophysitism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arianism" title="Arianism">Arianism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Paulicianism" title="Paulicianism">Paulicianism</a>, whose teachings were in some opposition to the main theological doctrine, as determined by the Ecumenical Councils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[195]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another division among Christians occurred, when Leo III ordered the destruction of icons throughout the Empire. This led to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_iconoclasm" title="Byzantine iconoclasm">significant religious crisis</a>, which ended in mid-9th century with the restoration of icons. During the same period, a new wave of pagans emerged in the Balkans, originating mainly from Slavic people. These were gradually <a href="/wiki/Christianization" title="Christianization">Christianised</a>, and by Byzantium's late stages, Eastern Orthodoxy represented most Christians and, in general, most people in what remained of the Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M115_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-M115-197">[196]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jews were a significant minority in the Byzantine state throughout its history, and, according to Roman law, they constituted a legally recognised religious group. In the early Byzantine period they were generally tolerated, but then periods of tensions and persecutions ensued. In any case, after the Arab conquests, the majority of Jews found themselves outside the Empire; those left inside the Byzantine borders apparently lived in relative peace from the 10th century onwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M114_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-M114-198">[197]</a></sup></p>
<p>Georgian monasteries first appear in <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> and on Mount Olympos in northwestern Asia Minor in the second half of the ninth century, and from then on <a href="/wiki/Georgians" title="Georgians">Georgians</a> played an increasingly important role in the Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[198]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_literature">Art and literature</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_art" title="Byzantine art">Byzantine art</a> and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_literature" title="Byzantine literature">Byzantine literature</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_dress" title="Byzantine dress">Byzantine dress</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RabulaGospelsFol13vAscension.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="793" data-file-width="611" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/RabulaGospelsFol13vAscension.jpg/220px-RabulaGospelsFol13vAscension.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/RabulaGospelsFol13vAscension.jpg/330px-RabulaGospelsFol13vAscension.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/RabulaGospelsFol13vAscension.jpg/440px-RabulaGospelsFol13vAscension.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RabulaGospelsFol13vAscension.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Miniatures of the 6th-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rabula_Gospel" title="Rabula Gospel">Rabula Gospel</a> display the more abstract and symbolic nature of Byzantine art.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Surviving Byzantine art is mostly religious and with exceptions at certain periods is highly conventionalised, following traditional models that translate carefully controlled church theology into artistic terms. Painting in <a href="/wiki/Fresco" title="Fresco">fresco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated manuscripts</a> and on wood panel and, especially in earlier periods, <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaic</a> were the main media, and figurative <a href="/wiki/Sculpture" title="Sculpture">sculpture</a> very rare except for small <a href="/wiki/Ivory_carving" title="Ivory carving">carved ivories</a>. Manuscript painting preserved to the end some of the classical realist tradition that was missing in larger works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[199]</a></sup> Byzantine art was highly prestigious and sought-after in Western Europe, where it maintained a continuous influence on <a href="/wiki/Medieval_art" title="Medieval art">medieval art</a> until near the end of the period. This was especially so in Italy, where Byzantine styles persisted in modified form through the 12th century, and became formative influences on <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a> art. But few incoming influences affected Byzantine style. By means of the expansion of the Eastern Orthodox church, Byzantine forms and styles spread to all the Orthodox world and beyond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[200]</a></sup> Influences from Byzantine architecture, particularly in religious buildings, can be found in diverse regions from Egypt and Arabia to Russia and Romania.</p>
<p>In Byzantine literature, four different cultural elements are recognised: the <a href="/wiki/Greek_literature" title="Greek literature">Greek</a>, the Christian, the <a href="/wiki/Latin_literature" title="Latin literature">Roman</a>, and the Oriental. Byzantine literature is often classified in five groups: historians and annalists, encyclopaedists (Patriarch Photios, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michael_Psellus" title="Michael Psellus">Michael Psellus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Choniates" title="Michael Choniates">Michael Choniates</a> are regarded as the greatest encyclopaedists of Byzantium) and essayists, and writers of secular poetry. The only genuine heroic epic of the Byzantines is the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digenis_Acritas" title="Digenis Acritas">Digenis Acritas</a></i>. The remaining two groups include the new literary species: ecclesiastical and theological literature, and popular poetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mango275.E2.80.93276_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mango275.E2.80.93276-202">[201]</a></sup></p>
<p>Of the approximately two to three thousand volumes of Byzantine literature that survive, only three hundred and thirty consist of secular poetry, history, science and pseudo-science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mango275.E2.80.93276_202-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mango275.E2.80.93276-202">[201]</a></sup> While the most flourishing period of the secular literature of Byzantium runs from the 9th to the 12th century, its religious literature (<a href="/wiki/Sermon" title="Sermon">sermons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_book" title="Liturgical book">liturgical books</a> and poetry, theology, devotional treatises, etc.) developed much earlier with <a href="/wiki/Romanos_the_Melodist" title="Romanos the Melodist">Romanos the Melodist</a> being its most prominent representative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CLi_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-CLi-203">[202]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_music" title="Byzantine music">Byzantine music</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright" style=";">
<div class="thumbinner" style="overflow:hidden;width:362px;">
<div class="thumbimage" style="height:262px; overflow:hidden; position:relative; background-color:white;">
<div style=";left:-210px; top:-100px; width:800px; position:absolute;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg" title='Late 4th century AD "Mosaic of the Musicians" with organ, aulos, and lyre from a Byzantine villa in Maryamin, Syria.[203]'><img alt="" data-file-height="1180" data-file-width="1678" height="563" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg/800px-Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg/1200px-Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg/1600px-Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg 2x" width="800"/></a></div>
<div style="text-align:left; background-color:transparent; line-height:110%;"></div>
<div style="visibility:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1180" data-file-width="1678" height="563" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg/800px-Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg/1200px-Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg/1600px-Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg 2x" width="800"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg" title="File:Mosaic of the Female Musicians.jpg"></a></div>
Late 4th century AD "Mosaic of the Musicians" with <a href="/wiki/Pipe_organ" title="Pipe organ">organ</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aulos" title="Aulos">aulos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lyre" title="Lyre">lyre</a> from a Byzantine villa in <a href="/wiki/Maryamin,_Hama" title="Maryamin, Hama">Maryamin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ring_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ring-204">[203]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The ecclesiastical forms of Byzantine music, composed to Greek texts as ceremonial, festival, or church music,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[204]</a></sup> are, today, the most well-known forms. Ecclesiastical chants were a fundamental part of this genre. Greek and foreign historians agree that the ecclesiastical tones and in general the whole system of Byzantine music is closely related to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_music" title="Ancient Greek music">ancient Greek system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[205]</a></sup> It remains the oldest genre of extant music, of which the manner of performance and (with increasing accuracy from the 5th century onwards) the names of the composers, and sometimes the particulars of each musical work's circumstances, are known.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_Lyra_Museo_Nazionale.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="378" data-file-width="400" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Byzantine_Lyra_Museo_Nazionale.jpg/160px-Byzantine_Lyra_Museo_Nazionale.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Byzantine_Lyra_Museo_Nazionale.jpg/240px-Byzantine_Lyra_Museo_Nazionale.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Byzantine_Lyra_Museo_Nazionale.jpg/320px-Byzantine_Lyra_Museo_Nazionale.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_Lyra_Museo_Nazionale.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Earliest known depiction of a <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_lyra" title="Byzantine lyra">bowed lyra</a>, from a Byzantine ivory casket (900 – 1100 AD). (<i>Museo Nazionale, Florence</i>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 9th century <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persian</a> geographer <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Khordadbeh" title="Ibn Khordadbeh">Ibn Khurradadhbih</a> (d. 911); in his lexicographical discussion of instruments cited the lyra (lūrā) as the typical instrument of the Byzantines along with the <i>urghun</i> (organ), <i>shilyani</i> (probably a type of <a href="/wiki/Harp" title="Harp">harp</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lyre" title="Lyre">lyre</a>) and the <i>salandj</i> (probably a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bagpipe" title="Bagpipe">bagpipe</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kartomi124_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kartomi124-207">[206]</a></sup> The first of these, the early bowed stringed instrument known as the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_lyra" title="Byzantine lyra">Byzantine lyra</a>, would come to be called the <i><a href="/wiki/Lira_da_braccio" title="Lira da braccio">lira da braccio</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[207]</a></sup> in Venice, where it is considered by many to have been the predecessor of the contemporary violin, which later flourished there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arkenberg109_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arkenberg109-209">[208]</a></sup> The bowed "lyra" is still played in former Byzantine regions, where it is known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Politiki_lyra" title="Politiki lyra">Politiki lyra</a> (lit. "lyra of the City" i.e. <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>) in Greece, the <a href="/wiki/Calabrian_lira" title="Calabrian lira">Calabrian lira</a> in Southern Italy, and the <a href="/wiki/Lijerica" title="Lijerica">Lijerica</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia" title="Dalmatia">Dalmatia</a>. The second instrument, the organ, originated in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic" title="Hellenistic">Hellenistic</a> world (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydraulis" title="Hydraulis">Hydraulis</a>) and was used in the <a href="/wiki/Hippodrome" title="Hippodrome">Hippodrome</a> during races.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bush-Kassel-327_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bush-Kassel-327-211">[210]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Pipe_organ" title="Pipe organ">pipe organ</a> with "great leaden pipes" was sent by the emperor <a href="/wiki/Constantine_V" title="Constantine V">Constantine V</a> to <a href="/wiki/Pepin_the_Short" title="Pepin the Short">Pepin the Short</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Frankish_kings#Kings_of_the_Franks" title="List of Frankish kings">King of the Franks</a> in 757. Pepin's son <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> requested a similar organ for his chapel in <a href="/wiki/Aachen" title="Aachen">Aachen</a> in 812, beginning its establishment in Western church music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bush-Kassel-327_211-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bush-Kassel-327-211">[210]</a></sup> The final Byzantine instrument, the <a href="/wiki/Aulos" title="Aulos">aulos</a> was a double reeded woodwind like the modern <a href="/wiki/Oboe" title="Oboe">oboe</a> or Armenian <a href="/wiki/Duduk" title="Duduk">duduk</a>. Other forms include the <i>plagiaulos</i> (<i>πλαγίαυλος</i>, from <i>πλάγιος</i> "sideways"), which resembled the <a href="/wiki/Flute" title="Flute">flute</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howard_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-Howard-212">[211]</a></sup> and the <i>askaulos</i> (ἀσκός <i>askos</i> – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wine-skin" title="Wine-skin">wine-skin</a>), a bagpipe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google15_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-google15-213">[212]</a></sup> These bagpipes, also known as <i><a href="/wiki/Dankiyo" title="Dankiyo">Dankiyo</a></i> (from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">ancient Greek</a>: angion (Τὸ ἀγγεῖον) "the container"), had been played even in Roman times. <a href="/wiki/Dio_Chrysostom" title="Dio Chrysostom">Dio Chrysostom</a> wrote in the 1st century of a contemporary sovereign (possibly <a href="/wiki/Nero" title="Nero">Nero</a>) who could play a pipe (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tibia_(instrument)" title="Tibia (instrument)">tibia</a>, Roman reedpipes similar to Greek aulos) with his mouth as well as by tucking a bladder beneath his armpit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[213]</a></sup> The bagpipes continued to be played throughout the empire's former realms through to the present. (See Balkan <a href="/wiki/Gaida" title="Gaida">Gaida</a>, Greek <a href="/wiki/Tsampouna" title="Tsampouna">Tsampouna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Greek" title="Pontic Greek">Pontic</a> <a href="/wiki/Tulum_(bagpipe)" title="Tulum (bagpipe)">Tulum</a>, Cretan <a href="/wiki/Askomandoura" title="Askomandoura">Askomandoura</a>, Armenian <a href="/wiki/Parkapzuk" title="Parkapzuk">Parkapzuk</a>, and Romanian <a href="/wiki/Cimpoi" title="Cimpoi">Cimpoi</a>.)</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_cuisine" title="Byzantine cuisine">Byzantine cuisine</a></div>
<p>The Byzantine culture was, initially, the same as Late Greco-Roman, but over the following millennium of the empire's existence it slowly changed into something more similar to modern Balkan and Anatolian culture. The cuisine still relied heavily on the Greco-Roman fish-sauce condiment <a href="/wiki/Garum" title="Garum">garos</a>, but it also contained foods still familiar today, such as the cured meat <a href="/wiki/Pastirma" title="Pastirma">pastirma</a> (known as "paston" in Byzantine Greek),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[214]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[215]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[216]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Baklava" title="Baklava">baklava</a> (known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Placenta_(food)" title="Placenta (food)">koptoplakous</a> κοπτοπλακοῦς),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[217]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tiropita" title="Tiropita">tiropita</a> (known as plakountas tetyromenous or tyritas plakountas),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[218]</a></sup> and the famed medieval sweet wines (<a href="/wiki/Commandaria" title="Commandaria">Commandaria</a> and the eponymous <a href="/wiki/Rumney_wine" title="Rumney wine">Rumney wine</a>). <a href="/wiki/Retsina" title="Retsina">Retsina</a>, wine flavored with pine resin, was also drunk, as it still is in Greece today, producing similar reactions from unfamiliar visitors; "To add to our calamity the Greek wine, on account of being mixed with pitch, resin, and plaster was to us undrinkable," complained <a href="/wiki/Liutprand_of_Cremona" title="Liutprand of Cremona">Liutprand of Cremona</a>, who was the ambassador sent to Constantinople in 968 by the German Holy Roman Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otto_I" title="Otto I">Otto I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fordham_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fordham-220">[219]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Garos" title="Garos">garos</a> fish sauce condiment was also not much appreciated by the unaccustomed; Liutprand of Cremona described being served food covered in an "exceedingly bad fish liquor."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fordham_220-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fordham-220">[219]</a></sup> The Byzantines also used a soy sauce like condiment, <a href="/wiki/Murri_(condiment)" title="Murri (condiment)">murri</a>, a fermented barley sauce, which, like soy sauce, provided <a href="/wiki/Umami" title="Umami">umami</a> flavoring to their dishes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[221]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recreation">Recreation</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tabula_-_boardgame_-_Zeno_game.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="303" data-file-width="585" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Tabula_-_boardgame_-_Zeno_game.svg/220px-Tabula_-_boardgame_-_Zeno_game.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Tabula_-_boardgame_-_Zeno_game.svg/330px-Tabula_-_boardgame_-_Zeno_game.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Tabula_-_boardgame_-_Zeno_game.svg/440px-Tabula_-_boardgame_-_Zeno_game.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tabula_-_boardgame_-_Zeno_game.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A game of <a href="/wiki/Tabula_(game)" title="Tabula (game)">τάβλι (tabula)</a> played by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> emperor <a href="/wiki/Zeno_(emperor)" title="Zeno (emperor)">Zeno</a> in 480 and recorded by <a href="/wiki/Agathias" title="Agathias">Agathias</a> in c. 530 because of a very unlucky dice throw for Zeno (red), as he threw 2, 5 and 6 and was forced to leave eight pieces alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Austin_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-Austin-223">[222]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Byzantines were avid players of <a href="/wiki/Tabula_(game)" title="Tabula (game)">tavli</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_Greek" title="Byzantine Greek">Byzantine Greek</a>: τάβλη), a game known in English as <a href="/wiki/Backgammon" title="Backgammon">backgammon</a>, which is still popular in former Byzantine realms, and still known by the name tavli in Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Austin_223-1"><a href="#cite_note-Austin-223">[222]</a></sup> Byzantine nobles were devoted to horsemanship, particularly <a href="/wiki/Tzykanisterion" title="Tzykanisterion">tzykanion</a>, now known as <a href="/wiki/Polo" title="Polo">polo</a>. The game came from Sassanid Persia in the early period and a Tzykanisterion (stadium for playing the game) was built by <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_II" title="Theodosius II">Theodosius II</a> (r. 408–450) inside the <a href="/wiki/Great_Palace_of_Constantinople" title="Great Palace of Constantinople">Great Palace of Constantinople</a>. Emperor <a href="/wiki/Basil_I" title="Basil I">Basil I</a> (r. 867–886) excelled at it; Emperor <a href="/wiki/Alexander_(Byzantine_emperor)" title="Alexander (Byzantine emperor)">Alexander</a> (r. 912–913) died from exhaustion while playing, Emperor <a href="/wiki/Alexios_I_Komnenos" title="Alexios I Komnenos">Alexios I Komnenos</a> (r. 1081–1118) was injured while playing with <a href="/wiki/Tatikios" title="Tatikios">Tatikios</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_I_of_Trebizond" title="John I of Trebizond">John I of Trebizond</a> (r. 1235–1238) died from a fatal injury during a game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[224]</a></sup> Aside from <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trabzon" title="Trabzon">Trebizond</a>, other Byzantine cities also featured <i>tzykanisteria</i>, most notably <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, an indication of a thriving urban aristocracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[225]</a></sup> The game was introduced to the West by crusaders, who developed a taste for it particularly during the pro-Western reign of emperor <a href="/wiki/Manuel_I_Komnenos" title="Manuel I Komnenos">Manuel I Komnenos</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_bureaucracy">Government and bureaucracy</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_bureaucracy" title="Byzantine bureaucracy">Byzantine bureaucracy</a></div>
<p>In the Byzantine state, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Byzantine_emperors" title="List of Byzantine emperors">emperor</a> was the sole and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absolute_ruler" title="Absolute ruler">absolute ruler</a>, and his power was regarded as having divine origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[226]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Senate" title="Byzantine Senate">Senate</a> had ceased to have real political and legislative authority but remained as an honorary council with titular members. By the end of the 8th century, a civil administration focused on the court was formed as part of a large-scale consolidation of power in the capital (the rise to pre-eminence of the position of <i><a href="/wiki/Sakellarios" title="Sakellarios">sakellarios</a></i> is related to this change).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L291_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-L291-228">[227]</a></sup> The most important administrative reform, which probably started in the mid-7th century, was the creation of <a href="/wiki/Theme_(Byzantine_district)" title="Theme (Byzantine district)">themes</a>, where civil and military administration was exercised by one person, the <i><a href="/wiki/Strategos" title="Strategos">strategos</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M60_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-M60-229">[228]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:428px;max-width:428px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:212px;max-width:212px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_Empire_Themata-750-en.svg"><img alt="Map of Byzantine Empire showing the themes in circa 750" data-file-height="457" data-file-width="743" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Byzantine_Empire_Themata-750-en.svg/210px-Byzantine_Empire_Themata-750-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Byzantine_Empire_Themata-750-en.svg/315px-Byzantine_Empire_Themata-750-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Byzantine_Empire_Themata-750-en.svg/420px-Byzantine_Empire_Themata-750-en.svg.png 2x" width="210"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The <i>themes</i>, c. 750</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:212px;max-width:212px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_Empire_Themata-950-en.svg"><img alt="Map of Byzantine Empire showing the themes in circa 950" data-file-height="455" data-file-width="747" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Byzantine_Empire_Themata-950-en.svg/210px-Byzantine_Empire_Themata-950-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Byzantine_Empire_Themata-950-en.svg/315px-Byzantine_Empire_Themata-950-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Byzantine_Empire_Themata-950-en.svg/420px-Byzantine_Empire_Themata-950-en.svg.png 2x" width="210"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The <i>themes</i>, c. 950</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite the occasionally derogatory use of the terms "Byzantine" and "<a href="/wiki/Byzantinism" title="Byzantinism">Byzantinism</a>", the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_bureaucracy" title="Byzantine bureaucracy">Byzantine bureaucracy</a> had a distinct ability for reconstituting itself in accordance with the Empire's situation. The elaborate system of titulature and precedence gave the court prestige and influence. Officials were arranged in strict order around the emperor, and depended upon the imperial will for their ranks. There were also actual administrative jobs, but authority could be vested in individuals rather than offices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L34_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-L34-230">[229]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 8th and 9th centuries, civil service constituted the clearest path to aristocratic status, but, starting in the 9th century, the civil aristocracy was rivalled by an aristocracy of nobility. According to some studies of Byzantine government, 11th-century politics were dominated by competition between the civil and the military aristocracy. During this period, Alexios I undertook important administrative reforms, including the creation of new courtly dignities and offices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L13_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-L13-231">[230]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diplomacy">Diplomacy</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_diplomacy" title="Byzantine diplomacy">Byzantine diplomacy</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_the_Grammarian_as_ambassador_before_Theophilos_and_Mamun.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="531" data-file-width="907" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/John_the_Grammarian_as_ambassador_before_Theophilos_and_Mamun.jpg/250px-John_the_Grammarian_as_ambassador_before_Theophilos_and_Mamun.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/John_the_Grammarian_as_ambassador_before_Theophilos_and_Mamun.jpg/375px-John_the_Grammarian_as_ambassador_before_Theophilos_and_Mamun.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/John_the_Grammarian_as_ambassador_before_Theophilos_and_Mamun.jpg/500px-John_the_Grammarian_as_ambassador_before_Theophilos_and_Mamun.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_the_Grammarian_as_ambassador_before_Theophilos_and_Mamun.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The embassy of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_John_VII_of_Constantinople" title="Patriarch John VII of Constantinople">John the Grammarian</a> in 829, between the emperor <a href="/wiki/Theophilos_(emperor)" title="Theophilos (emperor)">Theophilos</a> and the Abbasid caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27mun" title="Al-Ma'mun">Al-Ma'mun</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the fall of Rome, the key challenge to the Empire was to maintain a set of relations between itself and its neighbours. When these nations set about forging formal political institutions, they often modelled themselves on Constantinople. Byzantine diplomacy soon managed to draw its neighbours into a network of international and inter-state relations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-N1_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-N1-232">[231]</a></sup> This network revolved around treaty making, and included the welcoming of the new ruler into the family of kings, and the assimilation of Byzantine social attitudes, values and institutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[232]</a></sup> Whereas classical writers are fond of making ethical and legal distinctions between peace and war, Byzantines regarded diplomacy as a form of war by other means. For example, a Bulgarian threat could be countered by providing money to the <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus'</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ant_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ant-234">[233]</a></sup></p>
<p>Diplomacy in the era was understood to have an intelligence-gathering function on top of its pure political function. The <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Barbarians" title="Bureau of Barbarians">Bureau of Barbarians</a> in Constantinople handled matters of protocol and record keeping for any issues related to the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barbarians" title="Barbarians">barbarians</a>", and thus had, perhaps, a basic intelligence function itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[234]</a></sup> John B. Bury believed that the office exercised supervision over all foreigners visiting Constantinople, and that they were under the supervision of the <a href="/wiki/Logothetes_tou_dromou" title="Logothetes tou dromou">Logothetes tou dromou</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[235]</a></sup> While on the surface a protocol office – its main duty was to ensure foreign envoys were properly cared for and received sufficient state funds for their maintenance, and it kept all the official translators – it probably had a security function as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[236]</a></sup></p>
<p>Byzantines availed themselves of a number of diplomatic practices. For example, embassies to the capital would often stay on for years. A member of other royal houses would routinely be requested to stay on in Constantinople, not only as a potential hostage, but also as a useful pawn in case political conditions where he came from changed. Another key practice was to overwhelm visitors by sumptuous displays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-N1_232-1"><a href="#cite_note-N1-232">[231]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Dimitri_Obolensky" title="Dimitri Obolensky">Dimitri Obolensky</a>, the preservation of the ancient civilisation in Europe was due to the skill and resourcefulness of Byzantine diplomacy, which remains one of Byzantium's lasting contributions to the history of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O3_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-O3-238">[237]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flags_and_insignia">Flags and insignia</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_flags_and_insignia" title="Byzantine flags and insignia">Byzantine flags and insignia</a></div>
<p>For most of its history, the Byzantine Empire did not know or use <a href="/wiki/Heraldry" title="Heraldry">heraldry</a> in the West European sense. Various emblems (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">σημεία</span>, <i>sēmeia</i>; sing. σημείον, <i>sēmeion</i>) were used in official occasions and for military purposes, such as banners or shields displaying various motifs such as the <a href="/wiki/Cross" title="Cross">cross</a> or the <i><a href="/wiki/Labarum" title="Labarum">labarum</a></i>. The use of the cross, and of images of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christ" title="Christ">Christ</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a> and various saints is also <a href="/wiki/Sigillography" title="Sigillography">attested on seals</a> of officials, but these were personal rather than family emblems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKazhdan1991472.2C_999_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKazhdan1991472.2C_999-239">[238]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Byzantine_flags_and_insignia#Eagles" title="Byzantine flags and insignia">Double-headed eagle</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Byzantine_flags_and_insignia#Tetragrammic_cross" title="Byzantine flags and insignia">Tetragrammic cross</a></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Greek" title="Medieval Greek">Medieval Greek</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:408px;max-width:408px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mudil_Psalter.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="358" data-file-width="450" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Mudil_Psalter.jpg/200px-Mudil_Psalter.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Mudil_Psalter.jpg/300px-Mudil_Psalter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Mudil_Psalter.jpg/400px-Mudil_Psalter.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Joshua_Roll.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1365" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Joshua_Roll.jpg/200px-Joshua_Roll.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Joshua_Roll.jpg/300px-Joshua_Roll.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Joshua_Roll.jpg/400px-Joshua_Roll.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Left: The Mudil Psalter, the oldest complete <a href="/wiki/Psalter" title="Psalter">psalter</a> in the Coptic language (<a href="/wiki/Coptic_Museum" title="Coptic Museum">Coptic Museum</a>, Egypt, <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Cairo" title="Coptic Cairo">Coptic Cairo</a>).<br/>
Right: The <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Roll" title="Joshua Roll">Joshua Roll</a>, a 10th-century illuminated Greek manuscript probably made in Constantinople (<a href="/wiki/Vatican_Library" title="Vatican Library">Vatican Library</a>, Rome).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greek_Asia_Minor_dialects.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="268" data-file-width="287" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Greek_Asia_Minor_dialects.png/220px-Greek_Asia_Minor_dialects.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/86/Greek_Asia_Minor_dialects.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greek_Asia_Minor_dialects.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Distribution of Greek dialects in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> in the late Byzantine Empire through to 1923. <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek#Demotic" title="Modern Greek">Demotic</a> in yellow. <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Greek" title="Pontic Greek">Pontic</a> in orange. <a href="/wiki/Cappadocian_Greek" title="Cappadocian Greek">Cappadocian</a> in green. (Green dots indicate Cappadocian Greek speaking villages in 1910.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[239]</a></sup>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Apart from the Imperial court, administration and military, the primary language used in the eastern Roman provinces even before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="Decline of the Roman Empire">decline of the Western Empire</a> was Greek, having been spoken in the region for centuries before Latin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[240]</a></sup> Following Rome's conquest of the east its 'Pax Romana', inclusionist political practices and development of public infrastructure, facilitated the further spreading and entrenchment of Greek language in the east. Indeed, early on in the life of the Roman Empire, Greek had become the common language of the Church, the language of scholarship and the arts, and, to a large degree, the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> for trade between provinces and with other nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[241]</a></sup> Greek for a time became <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">diglossic</a> with the spoken language, known as <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine</a> (eventually evolving into <a href="/wiki/Demotic_Greek" title="Demotic Greek">Demotic Greek</a>), used alongside an older <a href="/wiki/Attic_Greek" title="Attic Greek">written form</a> until Koine won out as the spoken and written standard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[242]</a></sup></p>
<p>The use of Latin as the language of administration persisted until formally abolished by Heraclius in the 7th century. Scholarly Latin would rapidly fall into disuse among the educated classes although the language would continue to be at least a ceremonial part of the Empire's culture for some time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ApWr_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-ApWr-244">[243]</a></sup> Additionally, <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">Vulgar Latin</a> remained a minority language in the Empire, mainly along the Dalmatian coast (<a href="/wiki/Dalmatian_language" title="Dalmatian language">Dalmatian</a>) and among the Romanian peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[244]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many other languages existed in the multi-ethnic Empire, and some of these were given limited official status in their provinces at various times. Notably, by the beginning of the Middle Ages, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a> had become more widely used by the educated classes in the far eastern provinces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BJVe_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-BJVe-246">[245]</a></sup> Similarly <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Georgian_language" title="Georgian language">Georgian</a> became significant among the educated in their provinces,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[246]</a></sup> and later foreign contacts made <a href="/wiki/Old_Church_Slavonic" title="Old Church Slavonic">Old Church Slavic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Persian" title="Middle Persian">Middle Persian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">Arabic</a> important in the Empire and its sphere of influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[247]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aside from these, since Constantinople was a prime trading center in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Mediterranean_region" title="History of the Mediterranean region">Mediterranean region</a> and beyond, virtually every known language of the Middle Ages was spoken in the Empire at some time, even <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[248]</a></sup> As the Empire entered its final decline, the Empire's citizens became more culturally homogeneous and the Greek language became integral to their identity and religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[249]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Third_Rome" title="Third Rome">Third Rome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_scholars_in_the_Renaissance" title="Greek scholars in the Renaissance">Greek scholars in the Renaissance</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol7v.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="507" data-file-width="455" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol7v.jpg/220px-Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol7v.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol7v.jpg/330px-Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol7v.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol7v.jpg/440px-Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol7v.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol7v.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
King <a href="/wiki/David" title="David">David</a> in robes of a Byzantine emperor; miniature from the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Psalter" title="Paris Psalter">Paris Psalter</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Byzantium has been often identified with absolutism, orthodox spirituality, orientalism and exoticism, while the terms "Byzantine" and "Byzantinism" have been used as bywords for decadence, complex bureaucracy, and repression. In the countries of <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central</a> and Southeast Europe that exited the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">Eastern Bloc</a> in the late 1980s and early 1990s, the assessment of Byzantine civilisation and its legacy was strongly negative due to their connection with an alleged "Eastern authoritarianism and autocracy." Both Eastern and Western European authors have often perceived Byzantium as a body of religious, political, and philosophical ideas contrary to those of the West. Even in <a href="/wiki/History_of_modern_Greece" title="History of modern Greece">19th-century Greece</a>, the focus was mainly on the classical past, while Byzantine tradition had been associated with negative connotations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A1_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-A1-251">[250]</a></sup></p>
<p>This traditional approach towards Byzantium has been partially or wholly disputed and revised by modern studies, which focus on the positive aspects of Byzantine culture and legacy. <a href="/wiki/Averil_Cameron" title="Averil Cameron">Averil Cameron</a> regards as undeniable the Byzantine contribution to the formation of the medieval Europe, and both Cameron and Obolensky recognise the major role of Byzantium in shaping Orthodoxy, which in turn occupies a central position in the history and societies of Greece, Romania, Bulgaria, Russia, Georgia, Serbia and other countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[251]</a></sup> The Byzantines also preserved and copied classical manuscripts, and they are thus regarded as transmitters of the classical knowledge, as important contributors to the modern European civilization, and as precursors of both the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">Renaissance humanism</a> and the Slav Orthodox culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C311_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-C311-253">[252]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the only stable long-term state in Europe during the Middle Ages, Byzantium isolated Western Europe from newly emerging forces to the East. Constantly under attack, it distanced Western Europe from Persians, Arabs, Seljuk Turks, and for a time, the Ottomans. From a different perspective, since the 7th century, the evolution and constant reshaping of the Byzantine state were directly related to the respective progress of Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C311_253-1"><a href="#cite_note-C311-253">[252]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the conquest of Constantinople by the Ottoman Turks in 1453, Sultan <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mehmed_II" title="Mehmed II">Mehmed II</a> took the title "<i>Kaysar-i Rûm</i>" (the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish_language" title="Ottoman Turkish language">Ottoman Turkish</a> equivalent of <a href="/wiki/Caesar_(title)" title="Caesar (title)">Caesar</a> of Rome), since he was determined to make the Ottoman Empire the heir of the Eastern Roman Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[253]</a></sup> According to Cameron, regarding themselves as "heirs" of Byzantium, the Ottomans preserved important aspects of its tradition, which in turn facilitated an "Orthodox revival" during the <a href="/wiki/Post-communism" title="Post-communism">post-communist</a> period of the Eastern European states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C311_253-2"><a href="#cite_note-C311-253">[252]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Annotations">Annotations</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Romania" was a popular name of the empire used mainly unofficially, which meant "land of the Romans".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> After 1081, it occasionally appears in official Byzantine documents as well. In 1204, the leaders of the Fourth Crusade gave the name <i>Romania</i> to the newly founded Latin Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The term does not refer to modern <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<li id="cite_note-215"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-215">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFAsh1995">Ash 1995</a>, p. 224: "Having inherited pastirma from the Byzantines, the Turks took it with them when they conquered Hungary and Romania."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-216"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-216">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFDavidson2014">Davidson 2014</a>, "Byzantine cookery", pp. 123–124: "This is certainly true of Byzantine cuisine. Dried meat, a forerunner of the <i>pastirma</i> of modern Turkey, became a delicacy."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-217"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-217">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFDalbyBourbouKoderLeontsin.C4.932013">Dalby et al. 2013</a>, p. 81: "<i>paston</i> or <i>tarichon</i>...Cured meats were either eaten raw or cooked in <i>pasto-mageireia</i> with bulgur and greens, mainly cabbage."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-218"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-218">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFAsh1995">Ash 1995</a>, p. 223; <a href="#CITEREFFaas2005">Faas 2005</a>, p. 184; <a href="#CITEREFVryonis1971">Vryonis 1971</a>, p. 482.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-219"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-219">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFFaas2005">Faas 2005</a>, pp. 184–185; <a href="#CITEREFVryonis1971">Vryonis 1971</a>, p. 482; <a href="#CITEREFSalaman1986">Salaman 1986</a>, pp. 184.</span></li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Byzantine_studies.2C_resources_and_bibliography">Byzantine studies, resources and bibliography</span></h3>
<ul>
<li>Fox, Clinton R. <a class="external text" href="http://www.romanity.org/htm/fox.01.en.what_if_anything_is_a_byzantine.01.htm" rel="nofollow">What, If Anything, Is a Byzantine? (Online Encyclopedia of Roman Emperors)</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20080410123427/http://www.doaks.org/Byzantine.html" rel="nofollow">Byzantine studies homepage</a> at <a href="/wiki/Dumbarton_Oaks" title="Dumbarton Oaks">Dumbarton Oaks</a>. Includes links to numerous electronic texts.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.fordham.edu/halsall/byzantium/" rel="nofollow">Byzantium: Byzantine studies on the Internet</a>. Links to various online resources.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://homepage.mac.com/paulstephenson/trans.html" rel="nofollow">Translations from Byzantine Sources: The Imperial Centuries, c. 700–1204</a>. Online sourcebook.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.deremilitari.org/" rel="nofollow">De Re Militari</a>. Resources for medieval history, including numerous translated sources on the Byzantine wars.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.fordham.edu/halsall/sbook1c.html" rel="nofollow">Medieval Sourcebook: Byzantium</a>. Numerous primary sources on Byzantine history.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.univie.ac.at/byzantine/" rel="nofollow">Bibliography on Byzantine Material Culture and Daily Life</a>. Hosted by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Vienna" title="University of Vienna">University of Vienna</a>; in English.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.ellopos.net/elpenor/greek-texts/greek-resources-constantinople.asp" rel="nofollow">Constantinople Home Page</a>. Links to texts, images and videos on Byzantium.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://graal.org.ua/en/theodoro-principality" rel="nofollow">Byzantium in Crimea: Political History, Art and Culture</a>.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.oeaw.ac.at/byzanz/" rel="nofollow">Institute for Byzantine Studies of the Austrian Academy of Sciences (with further resources and a repository with papers on various aspects of the Byzantine Empire)</a></li>
</ul>
